LLITERARY NOTES.
Bookseller Melville, of Melbourne, in a paper
read before the Library Conference, expressed the
opinion that from the disappearance of “ the
native periodical of high and literary kind,” or
from the fact that Australian authors “publish
their books through English houses,” “ we are
gainers.” Because, he says, our books are thus
“part of the broad stream of English literature.”
To measure the folly of this statement, apply it
to America. Or what would Melville say to book-
buyers who had their books sent out direct from
London on the ground that they thus formed part
of the broad stream of English book-selling?
* * * *
From a recent Adelaide Advertiser :
.—On the 27th March, at , the dearly-
beloved husband of Elizabeth ——, of inflammation of
the bowels, aged 30 years and 9 months, leaving a wife
and four children to mourn their loss.
What is home without our Johnny?
None in this world can tell
Like his broken-hearted loved ones,
For they miss him everywhere.
Oh, bear me gently to the grave,
And keep the funeral slow ;
The angels sing where I am gone,
And you should sing below.
Humor is chiefly the perception of the incongruity
of ideas. lor various reasons the average woman
has fewer ideas—thought-conoepts— than the
averageman; therefore she has so many fewer
points d'appui on which the Spirit of Incongruity
may rest. Is her sense of humor to be improved
by packing her fuller of ideas? Do educated
women see a joke better than uneducated ? Or
(2) is it to be improved by strengthening her
mental force apart from enlarging her mental
area ? It is not enough to have the incongruous
ideas at hand : there needs some electricity to
combine them, some dynamic power to overcome
their inertia. Do women of good natural brain-
grasp see a joke quicker than the intellectually
deficient ?
* * * *
Kingsley’s energy is pulsating still. A N.S. W.
Member of Parliament—Mr. Copeland—moved
the other day to establish State clothing factories.
I got this idea from reading ‘Alton Locke,’ ”
he said. “Who wrote it?” queried somebody.
Mr. Copeland “thought it was Reade.” But if
people are going to forget the author, and remem-
ber only his work, of what use is it to
sell his sinews to be wise,
His teeth and bones to buy a name?
Of course, you cannot argue from a Member
of Parliament.
* * * *
. La Gallienne : “Length in literature is only
justified by depth. There is no excuse for it in
fiction but subtlety of character-drawing, or such
constant rip-rap of incident as we find in Reade’s
Cloister and the Hearth.” Guy do Maupassant
and Rudyard Kipling have lived in vain if the
tired reviewer may not take ad vantage of their
new formulae for the expression of human drama.”
* * * *
One hardl y knows “John Oliver Hobbes ”in
* The Herb-Moon,” her new story just com-
meneed in Chapman’s Magazine. There is hardly
a trace of epigram, and the development of the
story so far is almost tedious.
A Troop of Triolets.
ROSE-LEAVES.
Rose kissed me to-day,
Will she kiss me to-morrovf ?
Let it be as it may,
Rose kissed me to-day.
But the pleasure gi ves way
To a savour of sorrow ;
Rose kissed me to-day—
Will she kiss me to-morrow?
In the school of Coquettes
Madam Rose is a scholar;—
O, they fish with all nets
In the school of Coquettes !
When her brooch she forgets,
’Tis to show her new collar ;
In the school of Coquettes
Madam Rose is a scholar !
There’s a tear in her eye,—
Such a clear little jewel!
What can make her cry ?
There’s a tear in her eye.
“ Puck has killed a big fly,—
And it’s horribly cruel; ”
There’s a tear in her eye,—
Such a clear little jewel!
AUSTIN DOBSON.
TO AN AUTUMN LEAF.
Wee shallop of shimmering gold !
Slip down from your ways in the branches.
Some fairy will loosen your hold—
Wee shallop of shimmering gold—
Spill dew on your bows and unfold
Silk sails for the fairest of launches !
Wee shallop of shimmering gold,
Slip down from your ways in the branohes I
C. H. LUDES.
UNDER THE ROSE.
SAMUEL MINTURN PEOL.
He (aside).
If I should steal a little kiss,
Oh, would she weep, I wonder T
I tremble at the thought of bliss—
If I should steal a little kiss !
Sucli pouting lips would never miss
The dainty bit of plunder ;
If I should steal a little kiss,
Oh, would she weep, I wonder?
SHE (aside).
He longs to steal a kiss of mine—
He may, if he’ll return it:
If I can read the tender sign,
He longs to steal a kiss of mine ;
“ In love and war ” —you know the line—
Why cannot he discern it?
He longs to steal a kiss of mine—
He may if he’ll return it.
both (live minutes later).
A little kiss when no one sees,
Where is the impropriety?
How sweet amid the birds and bees
A little kiss when no one sees !
Nor is it wrong, the world agrees,
If taken with sobriety.
A little kiss when no one sees,
Where is the impropriety ?
LECON DE CHANT.
Moi, je regardais ce cou-lk
Maintenant chantez, me dit Paule.
Avee des mines d’Attila
Moi, je regardais ce cou-la.
Puis, un peu de temps s’ecoula. . .
Moi, je regardais ce cou-lil;
Maintenant chantez, me dit Paule.
Theodore de Banville.
Her lips were so near
That—what else could I do?
You’ll be angry, I fear,
Her lips were so near.
Well, I can’t make it clear
Or explain it to you.
Her lips were so near
That —what else could I do?
From The Century.
When Love is once dead,
Who shall awake him ?
Bitter our bread
When Love is once dead—
His comforts are fled,
His favors forsake him.
When Love is once dead
Who shall awake him ?
Arthur Symons.
Le premier jour du mois de mai
Fut le plus heureux de ma vie :
Le beau dessein que je formai,
Le premier jour du mois de mai !
Je vous vis et je vous aimai.
Si oe dassein vous plut, Sylvie,
Le premier jour du mois de mai
Fut le plus heureux de ma vie.—Ranohin.
Easy is the triolet,
If you really learn to make it I
Once a neat refrain you get,
Easy is the triolet,
As you see 1—I pay my debt
With another rhyme. Deuce take it,
Easy is the triolet,
If you really learn to make it!
W. S. HENLEY.
All the night and all the day
I think upon her lying dead,
With lips that neither kiss nor pray
All the night nor all the day.
In that dark grave whose only ray
Of sun or moon’s her golden head,
All the night and all the day
I think upon her lying dead.
A. MARY F. ROBINSON.
Spring sits on her nest,
Daisies and white clover 1
And young love lies at rest
In the Spring’s white nest,
For she loves me best,
And the cold is over ;
Spring sits on her nest,
Daisies and white clover.
GEORGE MACDONALD.
A pitoher of mignonette
In a tenement’s highest casement;
Queer sort of a flower-pot—yet
That pitcher of mignonette
Is a garden in heaven set
To the little sick child in the basement, —
The pitcher of mignonette
In the tenement’s highest casement.
H. C. BUNNER.

BEFORE YOU
L B A
When you go out shopping, consider, think
<>f the money in your pocket, and take account
of the many stores whose various attractions
are laid before you. There is A, with temp-
tation in the form of a pretty show ; there is
B, whose wiles are innumerable ; and C,
who says that he is the man for your money ;
and so on, throughout a list as long as
George-street, each smiling dealer has some-
thing to say and to offer.
Please Pause!
Hearken not to the song of the syren, sing
she ever so sweetly ; heed not the voice of
the charmer, charm he never so wisely.
Your pocket’s concerned ; and there are
pirates about. As the poet pertinently puts
it: “ There be land-sharks and water-
sharks,” and it is for you to see that your
dealings are with none of these voracious
monsters. Take warning in time, for it will
be no use when the new taxes come round—
and the tax-gatherer won’t take no for an
answer—for you to plead : There would have
been enough and to spare had I been wise
enough to

HOTEL
Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
GELIEBT UND VERLOREN.
Oeliebt und verloren : she stood foam-white
In the fragrant dusk of the Southern night,
Framed by the clinging passion-vine ;
And I see alway the dark eyes shine,
The dim verandah, the throbbing light
Of the stars that reeled in our reeling sight;
And across the long years’ leaden flight
A memory and a word are mine—
Oeliebt und verloren !
One year of blameless, pure delight,
Then the bolt from the blue (Heaven’s curse on its
might !);
A grave by the friendly sea is thine,
Its song thy dirge, and the breeze in the pine:
For me long thoughts that sting and bite —
Oeliebt und verloren.
N.S.W.
ROBET RICHARDSON.
“BY THE SOUND OF THE SEA.”
She was born, she was bred,
She was wooed, she was won, she was wed —
By the sound of the sea.
By the roar of its waves—
By its cliffs, and its caves,
She was loved, and was wed.
She is dead—she lies dead—
By the sound of the sea.
• • • • • •
“ Oh, where —oh, my Love !
Hast thou sped, hast thou fled ?
From the sound of the sea—
From the wash of its waves,
From its echoing caves—
Where, oh where, didst thou flee ?
“With seaweed for cypress,
I picture thy grave—
Silver sand for its turf,
Beaten down by the wave—
By the surge of the sea.
“Not long shall I linger,
Not long shalt thou wait;
She beckons, dear finger—
Dear finger of Fate.
Far away from the din,
From the crowd and the crush —
Where the tide cometh in
With a roar and a rush—
Oh, there make our grave,
Silver sand for our turf —
’Neath the crest of the wave,
By the sob of the surf. ”
Maoriland.
ZETA.
AND HE DID EAT.
1 “ The woman tempted me and I did eat.”— Genesis,
act Hi., scene 2.
And he did eat:
Let’s thank the Fates ’tis so,
For all the joys transcending that we know,
And haste to greet.
“ The woman tempted him ” —the words are writ,
And thus he fell away ;
So, with the beams of beauty, wiles of wit,
Her daughters tempt to-day.
And we do eat.
And he did eat :
A jealous God defied, ,
Who guessed that woman would His power divide,
If not defeat;
And, heeding not the threatened cost,
Eve to his bosom strained ;
What was the Paradise he lost
Beside the one he gained ?
So he did eat.
And he did eat:
So let us kiss and die,
And thank old Adam that to-day we lie
’Midst raptures sweet.
What would we give for such another curse ?
Who values aught above ?
What profits it to gain the universe
And taste not woman’s love ?
And so we eat.
Melb.
M.G.
IN A GULLY.
Rivulet! running with melodious fall
Under the shadow of down-drooping ferns,
Where aromatic breezes musical
Follow in playfulness thy mazy turns;
What if the fern should bid thy wave to stay,
To make delightful her half-hidden home?
Thou wouldst not tarry there, but glide away,
Forgetful of her fronds, afar to roam.
What if she droopeth fondly hour by hour,
Hoping to touch thee once ere thou canst go,
Shedding her garner from the Summer shower
In drops submissive to thine underflow ?
Thou dost not think of her, thou art not moved
By her mute invocation to thee bowed ;
So I, like her, have all too fondly loved
One too like thee, tameless and cold and proud,
And I would be upon thy bosom borne,
With my oppressive load of wretchedness
Slid from my heart, and with thee float forlorn
Into the ocean of forgetfulness.
Maori land.
H.C.
CONVALESCENT.
Let me lie in the sunshine and out of the wind
Here on the grass where the puppies play,
And the scent of the air from the barn behind
Is full of the fragrance of new-mown hay ;
While over my head the parroquets wheel,
Pleasant it is when thus reclined
To let youth’s sweet hours onward steal—
Warm in the sunshine and out of the wind.
Says a voice at my ear —as, slim and white,
A hand points out to the distant blue
“ There are prizes for those who share in the fight:
A prize perhaps may be won by you.”
But I : “ Ah, no ! let them fight who will :
Peace and warmth are more to my mind :
For you and me, let us linger still—
Warm in the sunshine and out of the wind.
“ Soon enough, dear, must everyone,
As fallen leaves and dead flowers teach,
Find the place where neither sun
Nor murmur of any wind can reach.
By us, as long as your lips are red,
And round my neck your arm is twined,
Let no other wisdom than this be said—
‘ Warm in the sunshine and out of the wind.
B.

SATURDAY, MAY 30, 1896.
The N.S.W. Political Outlook.
The N.S.W. Parliament resumed liusiness
lately with prospects of . a most interesting
session. The programme put forward by the
Reid Government is enormously long and
unwieldy, but there is much to be said for
it from a Democratic standpoint. Most of
the proposed measures are useful and pro-
gressive—or the}' should be so, unless the
ineptitude of the GoD-fearing Ministry, or
the opposition of the Ly>~e party, or the old
Calico-Jimmy party, or the Upper House, or
the morning shoddy organs, destroys their
usefulness in detail. Among the things pro-
mised are the restriction of colored immi-
gration. the locking of the Darling River so
as to provide a permanently-navigable water-
way and a permanent supply of water for
irrigation in the W est, and Acts to prevent
the adulteration of food and drink, to regu-
late factories and workshops, to abolish The
■■ truck system, to amalgamate the savings
banks into a State Bank of issue, to
build light railways, and to authorise the
acquisition of land for subdivision into
agricultural allotments—all of which are more
or less commendable if worked out on a
state.sm inlike basis. Also, it is proposed to
reform the L pper House, which is highly
desirable provided the reform is of a kind
tiiat will place that autocratic Chamber under
the control f>f the people ; and to establish a
modified Referendum applicable to “ im-
portant bills on which the two Houses have
disagreed in two consecutive sessions, " which,
at least, amounts to something, And, in
addition to these, a dozen or two other prac-
tical reforms are promised, including a bill
for the abolition of medical quacks," and a
measure for providing cheaper and more
easily accessible justice—which last is about
the most urgent and the least probable of
them all. The luckless Local Government
Bill is missing altogether ; but even without
it, if the Reid Ministry can carry out its
programme, and the programmes prove any-
thing like as good as it looks, X.S. Wales
should profit considerably in many ways.
The fact that the intentions of the Govern-
ment are good, and that those of the Assembly
seem to be moderately good, and that those
of the Council are apparently not nine
worse than usual, is something to begin with.
It remains to be seen, however, whether
there is sufficient intellect in the Govern-
ment and its following to carry out its plans
successfully. With the possible exception of
the Premier, the GoD-fearing Ministry is an
association of mediocrities of the most
mediocre description. The Foreign-trade
party in general is also dismally mediocre,
and it is to some extent divided against
itself, and it is also divided against its press
organs. The recent vanishment of W ise,
Dibbs, Parkes, Bartox, Slattery, Bruce
Smith, and others removed a lot of con-
spicuous ability— most of it ability of a kind
that did very little good to the country, yet
ability of a sort all the same. The present
is the Wilks and Whiddox and Parson
Smailes era—the period of much respectable
commonplace and much orthodox bun-
struggle. The M‘Millax section of the
Foreign-trade party is at one with the
Government about nothing except its
desire for Foreign-trade, and even its
brand of Foreign-trade is not the kind
which the Government advocates. The
freedom of trade which M Millax wants
is the free admission of softgoods, and
enough duties on other articles to preclude
any necessity for direct taxation, which falls
heavily on the importer’s profits. The
Government, too, is more or less divided
against itself ; the Reid end of it upholds
payment of members and threatens to demo-
cratise the L pper House therewith, and the
W axt end of it regards the same payment
as an invention of the devil and desires to
abolish i f utterly.
• » * »
The Labor-party of to-day is apparently
below the mental calibre of the original one
of a few years ago, and its leader is probably
one of the six dullest men in the Assembly.
A part of the Labor-party and a section of
the Freetraders are pledged to demand an
increase of the present land-tax, while the
Premier and at least one other member of
the Ministry are pledged to hold office in no
Cabinet which proposes to increase that tax
beyond its present figure. Meanwhile, it is
becoming more and more obvious that when
Freetrade really arrives—at present only an
instalment of it has come to hand—more
revenue must be obtained to fill the gap thus
created, and seeing that the Govt, is pledged
not to obtain it either by land sales or in-
creased land-tax only three courses seem to
be open. One is to pass a Local Govern-
ment Bill, and shift a lot of the expenditure
on to the local bodies, and keep the promise
in the letter while breaking it in the spirit
by making the increased land-tax a local
instead of a national affair. Another is to
fake ’ the public accounts by charging
ordinaiy expenditure to loans—the same
process by which Premier Reid squared his
first balance - sheet when he put nearly
£400,000 of borrowed money into revenue
on account of certain alleged transactions
dating many years back, and the process by
which he also squared his second balance-
sheet, when repairs to roads, bridges, and
old dredges were charged promiscuously to
loan-expenditure. The third possible* re-
source is to borrow money freely for “ light
railways," water conservation, and other
works, and thereby make an artificial burst
of prosperity which will inflate the existing
sources of revenue while it lasts, and leave
things more depressed than ever when it is
over.
* » * *
The Opposition, at all events, promises to
do neither much good nor much harm during
the present session. Almost all its bright
men were thrown out at the last elections,
while most of its preternaturally dull men
were returned by good majorities. The
hopeless and antiquated Copelaxd is fast
disrupting what remains of the partv by
endeavouring to jump the position of leader,
now held by the equally antiquated and hope-
less Lyxe. The latter politician, by the
sheer habit of opposing all the proposals of
the Government, has committed himself and
his Parliamentary followers to a policy of
old crusted Toryism, and thereby discredited
them with the country at large, and the man
who wants to succeed him has so far coun-
tenanced his action. Still, The Bulletix
trusts that Copelaxd will continue to struggle
for the leadership to the utmost of his ability,
and disrupt the Protectionist-party in the
Assembly all he knows. It is practically cer-
tain that he will never become leader, but in
the general upheaval and free-light which will
ensue it is just possible that the new man who
has so long been wanted may come to the
front. Who that new man may be it is at
present almost impossible to guess. Amon®
all the three parties there is not just now
visible one individual who looks like a com-
manding statesman. Even such a man as
Premier Turxer. of Victoria, whose level-
headedness. intense industry and great capa-
city for detail make a gpod substitute for
genius, can't be found at present in the
.X.S.W. Legislature. The Government is
loose and eloquent and sloppy and well-
meaning, and its measures are wonderful
examples of well-intentioned incomprehensi-
bility. And the balance of the House Ls
mostly beneath contempt, or somewhere
thereabouts.
•.* * *
The Legislative Council is in the main as
bitterly opposed to reform as ever. The ten
new members whom Premier Reid sent up
there last year are too few and weak to seri-
ously improve the tone of the Tory Cham-
ber, and even they have supported the
Government none too well. The Council is
not Protectionist, nor is enthusiastic about
genuine Freetrade ; it rather wants the old
revenue-tariff system of the M 'Millay days,
and no direct taxation, and peace and gentle
stagnation. The Government s representa-
tive in the Council. Attorney-General Wavt,
is utterly at variance with the new Demo-
cratic programme of his alleged party. The
alleged Government organ, the Daily Tele-
graph. supports the Ministry on its Foreign-
trade policy and curses every* separate Demo-
cratic item of its programme. It does not
definitely curse these items in the Reid pro-
gramme, but when many similar proposals
were advanced by Kingston in S.A. and by
•Seddon in Maori land and by Turner in
V ictoria, it denounced them unsparingly, so
its attitude is sufficiently e'xplicit. The S.M.
Herald, as the inferior Government organ,
mildly supports the Government’s Foreign-
trade policy and feebly “ deprecates ” every-
thing else. The Opposition organ denounces
everything the Government says or does or
is and acknowledges not one redeeming
feature in it. The Labor-parcy claims that
it is the power which owns and drives
dhe Reid Ministry, but the Labor-
party isn’t strong enough to form a
Ministry by itself, and it can’t sell
itself to Lyne now that Lyne has sold
himself to the L pper House and to old bar-
nacled Toryism, so it must support Reid in
the meantime, and therefore Reid owns it
body and soul—or very nearly so. Mean-
while, Reid, by posing as the democratic
leader of the Assembly, is cutting the ground
from under the feet of the Labor members.
They* could show a reason for their separate
existence when they were a democratic frag-
ment raising a voice of protest against the
stagnant order of things as represented by
Parkes on one hand and Dibbs on the other.
They can show much less reason now, when
they have principally to say “ ditto ” to
Reid ; yet in the order of things there is
nothing else which they can say. They might
begin a new separate existence as the party*
of financial reform—the jiarty which sets its
face hard against furilier borrowing, especi-
ally foreign borrowing, and demands that all
public works shall in future be built out of
revenue ; but there is at present no sign of
this. There is abundant room for such a
party in a Legislature where the finance of
all parties is about equally slipshod ; and by-
assuming the role, the Labor-men would do
a vast national service. Also, they would
thereby keep the country* from forgetting
their separate existence and their claims
upon the public gratitude. Too much ditto
to the party* which has borrowed their prin-
ciples will end sooner or later in the country-
forgetting whose principles they originally
were.
♦ » » «
The present is obviously a transition period
in X.S.M . politics. All periods are so more
or less, but the present is more definitely a
period of transition than most others. The
combination of Reid, M‘Millay, Want,
the old revenue-tariffists, the new Foreign-
traders and the Labor-party, can’t last.
Somebody must needs go out, and who it
will be, and where he will go, and what he
will do when he gets there, wherever it may
be, are interesting questions. The Reid
Government, the Radical Labor-party, and
the Conservative Daily* Telegraph' can’t
always keep on pretending to be one party
and one interest. The present Protec-
tionist party can’t last; the Protectionists
in the country, who are as democratic
as any other class, can’t be dragged for ever
at the tail of the Conservative section merelv
because Lyne has drifted into that unfor-
tunate situation and doesn’t know how to
get out of it. The alliance of Crick, the
more or less radical Protectionist, and Lyne
and Copeland, the Tory Protectionists,
doesn t look as if it could endure much longer,
and something must happen to the Upper
House if the Reid programme is to be carried
out, or to the Reid Government if it isn’t.
Further, the country can't live for ever on
borrowed money and on humping ashore
foreign-made goods at Circular Quav. Sydney,
so altogether the next great shuffle of the
political cards should be interesting.

The Unmarried FPublicaness.
The licensing courts of Xew South Wales have
practically ukased that no public-house licenses
shall be granted to unmarried women. Only
one unmarried woman, it appears, now holds a
license in the province, and every aDplication by
a spinster is flatly declined. 'The Bclletix
respectfully asks the “ Binch ' to give reasons
for its conduct in thi3 matter, seeing that it
allows widows and married women to hold
licenses, and permits spinsters to officiate as
barmaids, and also permits the publican's un-
married daughter to be about the premises.
It can t act as it does on the ground that the
publican needs to be a muscular person capable
of doing his own chucking out. for a widow
can't chuck out any better than a spinster, and,
besides, many male publicans are so small’or so
old that any ordinary unmarried woman of 25
or so could throw them out bodily with one
hand. It can t lie because keeping a pub
might lead the unmained publicaness into
drinking habits, else the bench would raise its
voice again the widowed publicaness also who
is quite as likely to be led astray, and against
the male drink-seller’s wife and daughter and
barmaid, and all his female belongings. It is
impossible to assume that the refusal is based
on the assumption that the drink-seller has to
listen to mucli language that is quite unlit for
publication, because the unmarried Miss Pcblic-
HorsE would, in most cases, simply put a spin-
ster barmaid behind her bar to 'listen to the
language, just as the male Mr. PcßLiCHorst-
now does. An unmarried female has to listen
to most of the language in any case. Possibly
the bench thinks that a licensee should be \
person who can curse back at the obstreperous
customer, but in many hotels the licensee is
seldom about, and, in any case, a spinster can
curse back quite as well as a widow, and both ol
them can curse at second-hand per medium of the
boots or the handy man, and, moreover, the
Bench shouldn’t countenance cursing in any
case. And if. as seems possible, it refuses to
countenance the maiden grog-dealer for the
alleged reason that the business tends to im-
morality, then it has no right to license the
widow, and the man who keeps his daughters
on the premises, and the man who employs
barmaids and thus exposes them to temptation.
When the barmaid does “go wrong,” her
seducer is as often the boss publican as any-
body else, and the male licensee is a cause of
immorality rather than a protection against it.
The only result of the present policy is that the
unmarried woman has to sell herself into slavery
by getting her license through a dummy, or a
brewer’s nominee, and keeping him on the pre-
mises as a guarantee of good faith, or she has
to marry some more or less objectionable person
to qualify herself, or she has to become the
rack-rented sub-tenant of a private bar. There
doesn’t appear on the surface of things one
reason for the present policy which can stan<T
inspection, and if the licensing benches have a
reason they ought to be made to explain it
forthwith.
W A. Land-Grant Hailways.
If any Australian province is still unconvinced
of the foolishness of the land-grant railway
system the experience of Westralia should
bring conviction. Natural conditions made
Westralia a happy hunting-ground for the land-
grant railway promoter.' The extent of the
province made railways particularly necessary
for its development, and a few million acres
more or less seemed of no particular account.
The population, being small, hesitated to build
extensive lines when private companies would
undertake the work for so much mere laud. So
private enterprise was given practically a free
hand. In 1884 the late Anthony Hordern
contracted to build a line from Albany to
Beverley (on the Govt. Eastern Railway), a
distance of 242 miles, for a subsidy of 12,000
acres per mile selected east and west of’the
line within a belt of 40 miles on each side, the
total subsidy thus being nearly three million
acres of land. The province did not get its
line as soon as it had hoped, for the contractor
sold his concession to the W.A. Land Co.,
which transfer the Govt, had to pass an Act to
confirm, and the line was not opened for traffic
till 1889. five years after the first contract was
made. Nevertheless Westralia was grateful,
as w itness an obelisk at Albany recording the
grat it uae of the peopleof theprovince to Anthony
Hordern for promoting the scheme. The co.
had only one small difficulty with the Govt.—
when they set forth to take possession of all
the unsold lots in the towns along the lines,
which undoubtedly the terms of the concession
gave them power to do. Then the Government
played a trump-card by declaring all the said
lots Government reserves. In 1896 the W.A.
Land Co. possesses a handsomely-paying trunk
line and is one of the biggest landlords ld the
province. It has sold some land, has an agri-
cultural tenantry on some more, and can afford
to wait while the rest is increasing in value,
which, being some of the best agricultural land
in \\ .A., it is doing rapidly. Omitting some-
thing like a score of timber concession and other
lines, the province’s other principal experiment
in land-grant railways is the Midland line,
280 miles in length, connecting the Eastern
and Northern Government systems. The
Midland scheme was launched in 1890.
The line cost £1,062,000, and the com-
pany received 3,360,000 acres of land, the
best of which was selected, alternately with
the Government, in blocks of 12,000 acres, with
five miles frontage to the line. The co. got
into difficulties before the line was finished, and
the Government had to come to the rescue with
a guarantee of £60,000. Now Westralia is
thinking hard over the bargains made, and the
province, having given six million acres of the
best land in the country away to induce two
co’s to build over 500 miles of paying line,
would like it back again. A deputation, com-
prising principally settlers on the Southern line,
has already asked Government to buy back that
line and the balance of the co s grant of land.
The Govt, is sympathetic, but is wondering how
it can be done. Westralia"s credit is good at
present, but probably not to the extent of the
millions which would be required to buy the
Southern and Midland lines and the territory
so recklessly squandered in connection with
their construction.
Overstocked Squattages.
The position of pastoral lands in the Western
Division of N.S. Wales wants enquiring into
closely (writes a Bulletin correspondent in the
Far YV est). One after the other pastoral lessees
have come up and sworn that the carrying
capacity of their country has depreciated any-
thing from 20 to SO per eent. The average
depreciation is stated to be 45 per cent. Not-
withstanding that, the stock-returns show only
a small reduction in the uumber of sheep carried
w lieu they show any reduction at all. In many
cases where a 50 per cent, reduction in the
carrying-capacity had beeu sworn to before the
appraising Land Boards, the stock-returns have
shown that there were more sheep on the land
at the end of last year than there were six
years ago. The explanation given is that it
pays better to risk losing a verv large per-
centage of the flocks in bad 'seasons for
the sake of the extra wool that can be
got in good seasons by overstocking. The
argument runs something in this way : “I
ought to carry only 50,000 sheep, but I am
carrying 100,000. If I only carried 50,000 I
should lose perhaps 2500 in a fair year." Sheep
die just as men do—a fact that city statisticians
never seem to take into account.) “ With
100,000 I lose 5000 in a fair year: in a bad
year, perhaps one in three, I lose 25,000. But
what does that matter ? I have had for two
years the wool for 50.000 sheep more than I
ought to have carried and from 25,000 for the


third year. Why, even if I lost the other
25,000 the next year, I would still be to the
g >od, because I can’t average more for a sheep
than I can average for the wool off
a sheep’s back for a single year.” In these
circumstances the sympathy expended upon
the squatter is largely uncalled-for. Moreover,
the figures showing the steady increase in the
stock losses annually are not as significant as
they are sometimes represented—at least, they
are significant in another way. It might reason-
ably be objected, also, that it is hardly a decent
thing to overcrowd country with the full
knowledge that whenever a bad season comes
there will be tremendous mortality among the
flocks through starvation. If the thing hap-
pened in the city the Society for the Preven-
tion of Cruelty to Animals would be around
very speedily. But there is another considera-
tion of perhaps greater moment to the public, to
whom those Western Division lands still belong.
How does country get along that is continually
overstocked? The lands are, generally speaking,
fully stocked in good seasons, and terribly over-
stocked in bad. That is, in the good seasons,
when the country ought to be recovering, it is
not given the opportunity to do so. The con-
sequence is the disappearance of most of the
best fodder-plants, and the survival and spread
■of the useless, often poisonous, weeds which,
;not being fed down, mature and seed. Millions
of acres of country are thus being devastated ;
millions of acres of the public estate are being
.either ruined or permanently injured. Mean-
time, from the sale of wool taken from the
illegitimately-grazed sheep, the Western Divi-
sion squatlages (to a smaller extent, also, those
iin the north-west of the Central Division) arq
nnade to appear very fair assets in the hands of
it lie banks and mortgage companies into which
they have fallen. The thing cannot last, how-
ever. Sheep losses must increase, and the
quality and quantity of wool and frame still
further decline. The policy is only one of
desperation, and is, besides being shortsighted
and also unfair to the landlord, cruel into the
bargain.
The Gaol Deficit.
Judge Hood, of Victoria, struck a great truth,
or at least a small part of it, the other day in a
Melb. robbery case. Prosecutor had come to
town for his annual or semi-annual or quarterly
drunk, and having duly made a beast of him-
self he zig-zagged down a dark lane, where
another man met him and cleaned him out.
The police speiu a good deal of valuable time
hunting for this other man, and when they
got a person who seemed like him he was
acquitted, after a trial that cost about £2O,
for want of sober and reliable evidence,
either for or against him. Whereupon Judge
Hood expressed the opinion, in an irritated
itone, that the law should make all Such prose-
.cutors pay the cost of the trial, on the ground
(that a man who deliberately made a beast of
,himself invited robbery, and thereby was acces-
sory to running the State into great expense.
This theory opens up another and still larger
.question : Why should not the criminal law be
made self-supporting in all its branches? The
■cost of police, gaols, criminal courts, &c., which,
iin New South Wales, runs to over £500,000 a
year, is all incurred through law-breakers, and
therefore it should be the object of every
(Government which wants to be solvent to make
the law-breakers pay for them. As it is they
only pay 8 per cent, of the amount, or less, leav-
ing the other 92 per cent, to be paid by the
people who don’t break the law, which is a
wholly ridiculous state of things. In fact, the
law fines the decent, well-behaved citizen, in
many cases, a great deal more heavily than it
does the habitual criminal, and no one seems to
notice the foolishness of this awful discrepancy.
The present system prescribes a maximum
penalty for each offence, but in most cases it
fixes no minimum, so the average easy-going
magistrate lets the average offender off at ss.
for an aggravated drunk and 40s. for a brutal
assault, and (leaving the question of justice out
.altogether) these rates don’t nearly pay ex-
penses. If the prisoner is sent to gaol the
case is still worse; he is then a dead loss
ifrom beginning to end, as his work
uvhile in confinement seems to be of no value
worth mentioning. Obviously this state of
ithings requires a remedy. When the law
sends a wealthy offender to prison it ought also
ito fine him enough to pay for the use of the
establishment; this may seem hard, but when
the alternative lies between making the law-
breaker or the law-keeper pay the bill, it is
obvious that the former should do so. Also,
some better effort should be made to introduce
paying industries into the gaols and reforma-
tories. And, in addition to this, the scale of
fines wants to be revised on such a liberal scale
that the man who is run in and convicted of a
fineable offence shall pay for the cost of the
policeman, and the lock-up, and his board in
the lock-up while awaiting trial, and the use of
the court-house and magistrate, and something
towards the support of the gaol which has to
be kept up for his benefit in case he should get
worse and go beyond the fineable limit. This
would probably mean raising the minimum
penalty for a very ordinary drunk to £2, and
making £lO the very lowest charge lor a
moderate assault, and fixing the smallest fine
for running an illegal tote at £3OO, and
levying £2O all round for a free fight at Chowder
on a holiday, and charging the influential lawyer
who is let out under the First Offenders Act
£l5O in costs, and doing many other disagree-
able things ; but even this is putting the crime
in at c< si price, and either the fraudulent lawyer
has to pay for his own conviction, or else a
large number of poor but honest people have
to do so on his account. In any case, some-
body has to pay, and the awful deficit has to
be considered. The seven provinces have all
worried much of late years over the Railway
shortage and the Post Office shortage and other
things, while the gaol and police shortage,
which is larger in many cases than all the others
put together, has passed quite unnoticed.
The Law of Seduction.
In another just-settled Melb. jury action, the
glaring anomalies of the suit for seduction have
been laid bare. Apart from the particular
merits of the parties, the case turned on the
question whether the girl could be said to have
been in the service of her father at the moment
of seduction. (This raised another point, viz.,
when was the moment of seduction—the first
fall from virtue ? or the actual cauxa causans of
the lying-in?- but this by the way). A few
illustrations as to what is being in the service
of one’s father and what is not will show the
delightful common sense of this branch of the
law. Where a daughter is in service but
occasionally visits home and is seduced while
on one of these visits, no action will lie. Where
she is in service but sleeps at home, the action
will usually lie. Where she has just left service
and is seduced on her way home, the logical
law puts her by implication in her father’s ser-
vice and gives her father a right of action. If
she lives with her brother but goes every day
to her father’s to do household work, she is
in his service in the meaning of (his law.
In fact, if she is married and living
under her father’s roof and is seduced (?)
he (not her husband) can recover on the ground
that her subsequent confinement, &e., has de-
prived him of services he has no right to. Such
is law. If a parent is well enough off to keep
his daughter at home, and she is seduced, he
can get aggravated damages, for this is one of
the actions in which “station in life” comes
into play. If she is away from home to help
eke out his earnings, he gets nothing if she is
seduced—say, by her employer. Surely the
days of this miserable fiction, “ loss of ser-
vices,” are about over. Give the girl an action
for seduction as in some American states, or
extend the “ maintenance ” law, or give the
father his action on an all-round, fair, demo-
cratic basis, or abolish the distinction between
legitimacy and illegitimacy (which Nature
knows nothing of), or abolish marriage, or take
away the remedy altogether, but, for the sake
of logic and fairness and the sense of humor,
get rid of this ridiculous figure-head of English
prudery, the action quart servitium ami-sit.
Prison Labor.
Item from the new Butler divorce-case :
Mr. Gannon made an application that the case should
be set down about two months ahead, as Butler had just
served his term of solitary confinement; as was usual in
the case of prisoners who had been subjected to this, his
mind and memory had become somewhat affected, and it
took some time before contact with the other prisoners
could restore him mentally.
This is interesting. In other words, the soli-
tary cell is a place for the manufacture of semi-
idiots—people who have vacant stares and
irresponsible gibbers, and who jump at sudden
noises, and grab at the atmosphere for things
that aren’t there, and hang their hands in front
of them when they walk, and shamble feebly
with their legs. A solitary prisoner does no
work, therefore he produces nothing to pay for
his board and lodging. The State pays a large
sum to make him an utterly unsaleable idiot—a
person who is of as little value as possible to
anybody; yet it doesn’t want its own idiot
when he is made, and has no earthly use for
him, and it is always lamenting about the
cost of manufacturing him. There is ob-
viously a weak point in such a system some-
where. One of the principal burdens of the
State is the cost of maintaining gaols, and
one way of reducing this cost is by getting the
largest possible amount of profitable work out
of the prisoners. The community wants this
done, and yet v lienever any attempt is made
at doing it the same community objects on the
ground that gaol-products should not be allowed
to compete with free labor, and thus reduce
wages. Under the Freetrade policy the pro-
ducts of German and Belgian gaols compete
largely with those of N.S. Wales free labor,
and thus reduce local wages, and no attempt is
made to prevent them doing so, but when the
products of local gaols are found doing the
same thing on any scale worth mentioning there
is wild upheaval and riot, and the system is
promptly squelched. The Bulletin would
like to suggest certain reforms in the present
slipshod system. One is that “solitary”
should be abolished —except as a means of sup-
pressing insubordination—on the ground that
it is stupid and brutal, that it produces
nothing save dementia, which isn’t a sale-
able article, and that it leaves idiots and
invalids on the hands of the State to be a
future burden on the revenue. Another is
that N.S. Wales, seeing that it admits foreign
gaol-products freely, might as well give up try-
ing to draw the line at the local article, and
establish in its gaols any industries which don’t
require too much technical skill, and sell the
products in the open market for the benefit of
the revenue. Then, if it employed all prisoners
continuously instead of leaving them to moulder
away for a great part of their time in solitary
confinement, and stimulated them to industry
by allowing each man a small percentage of the
value of his work, with the privilege of spend-
ing it on tobacco or liquor (within certain limits)
or books or other indulgences, it would make
the gaol system a little less expensive and a
great deal less antiquated and barbarous than
it is at present. The number of prisoners is
small at the best, and the possible effects of
prison competition amount to much less than
is commonly supposed, and, in any case, it is
every man’s right to labor, and there is nothing
in the criminal code which takes away that
right. And if gaol-products are abnormally
cheap, N.S. Wales, at all events, has no right
to object on that score. It has voted per-
sistently for Freetrade on the ground that it
wants cheap products.
Adulteration, Shoddy, and False
Pretences.
One of the measures promised this session by
the N.S. W. Government is the comprehensive
Adulteration Act which is so much needed in
the Land of Shoddy. When it comes it will,
The Bulletin hopes, put right at least one old-
established anomaly specially noticeable in the
liquor business. In that trade more than any
other the retailer is .made a scapegoat for the
wholesaler. The smaller dealer in Australian
wine, for example, generally buys the fluid
ready bottled, corked, sealed, and labelled, and
he sells it in that—condition. He can’t tell
what is in the bottles unless he gets the contents
of one of them subjected to an expensive analysis,
which he probably can’t afford to do, and, even
if he does, lie thereby only learns for certain
the nature of the contents of one bottle, and the
rest of the stock remains a mystery, as before.
Yet the law holds him responsible for the
quality of the goods, and fines him if they are
adulterated, and never, or hardly ever, attacks
the wholesale person, who is generally the
responsible party. It does the same with the
owner of the “ tied ” public-house, though he
has to take any liquer which the brewer or
spirit-merchant—who owns him, body, soul,
house, clothes, and boots—chooses to send him.
For some occult reason, it always, or nearly
always, assumes that the man who sells direct
to the public is the guilty party, even
when it is almost a matter of certainty that he
isn’t, and thereby it not only shows itself to
be unfeeling and unjust, but it also misses its
chance to put down the evil it complains of.
The retailer often enough desires to suppress
adulteration, because he is the person who suffers
by it, but in many cases he has to buy from a
dishonest wholesale person, and in others he
can’t find one who isn’t dishonest, and in most
cases he doesn’t know enough to tell whether
the goods he receives are dishonest or not.
Also, if the promised measure is to be a genuine
attempt to extinguish adulteration, it needs to
go beyond the mere question of food and drink
—the point at which most adulteration-laws
stop short. It must heavily penalise the man
who sells “all-wool” goods which are not all
wool, and who sells anything at all which isn’t
what it pretends to be ; also the person who
advertises his own old goods as “ bankrupt
stock ” or as salvage from a sensational fire or
shipwreck—in fact, it wants to attack the whole
question of shoddy and false pretences and
trade dishonesty, or, at least, as much of it as
possible. Adulteration, in the complete sense
of the term, consists in selling or offering for
sale any article which is represented to be what
it is not, and any law which means business will
endeavor to deal with it on that basis.
Writing to a N.S.W. correspondent, Q. ex-
Chief Justice Lilley once more points out the
risks of the British connection—that astound-
ing folly of which only blinding habit could
make Australia guilty :
We depend for peace through our link with Great Bri-
tain on the condition of some madman or fool who has
inherited a European or Oriental throne ! Have we not
fools enough and madmen to drive us to difficulty, danger,
and disaster without continuing the hazard of a plunge
by some distant madman in blood-shedding, in which we
have not even the poor defence of a common interest ?
Why depend for our peace on the Sultan’s pleasure, or a
Fatima or Haidee, or on a “ lapse by Ayesha,” or on the
Czar’s liver or surfeit of cabbage-soup, or on the divine
German Wilhelm’s disgust with “ das Englischer blut” in
his Imperial veins ? It seems absurd that a brave
offshoot from the old English race, with the Scottish
and Celtic mixture, should be at the mercy of
these foreign elements for his right to live in peace
and work out his own great future. Everything
points to the necessity for Australian independent nation-
hood, charged with no further alliance except, perhaps,
for the preservation of the sanctity of the soil of Great
Britain and Ireland from foreign pollution. The latest
scheme for trade and immigration means the discharge
from Russia, Germany, &c., of the alien pauperism which
is crushing the workers of Great Britain. What a precious
mixture we should be —Chinamen, Japs., Hindoos, Kaffirs
and German and Polish Jews, &c., &c. ! We have enough
to do to purge out the convict taint from Australia with-
out entering on the benevolent work of relieving Europe
of her surplus poverty and crime ! Australians should
look steadily to their own interests, and by no means
allow any obstacle to be placed between them and the
government of their own affairs in an independent
nationality.
A Bulletin correspondent on Vandemonian
politics :
The difference between the Ins and the Outs in Tas.
Parliament is more personal than political. The present
Braddon-Fysh Govt, advocates and collects an Income-
tax, a tax on the capital value of land, exempting im-
provements, and doesn’t shut down on the consultation
promoter or blame Braddon overmuch for attaching his
name to the prospectus of the Anchor tin-mine. The
Dobson-Lewis (dual leaderships are fashionable just now)
Opposition is understood to barrack for a graduated tax
on land and improvements, opposes the Income-tax
apologetically (as Ministers apologise for not opposing it),
is silent re consultations, and attacks the Premier
personally for dragging his name, as Premier,
in the mire of the Anchor muddle. Politically,
there is no difference worth mentioning in the
two programmes, especially as neither side has
ever made clear to the common people what
brand of land-tax either really aims at. The only
apparent chance of an agitation of the stagnant pool of
Tas. politics lies in Opposition-leader Dobson’s latest
declaration for a wealth-tax. This declaration, it is fair
to say, was made while endeavoring to explain the
benefits of his particular brand of Land-tax such
a hopeless task that the National Ass catch-word
proved a godsend to the befogged politician talking
to a still more befogged audience—and modifications
of the statement may be confidently expected. It is
hardlv reasonable to suppose that any political party in Tas.
Legislature would so far depart from ancient tradition as
to adopt a programme involving direct and violent oppo-
sition to any other party in the House, but if the Opposi-
tion really means what it says, and is prepared to pin its
faith to the wealth-tax advocated by the various National
Asses, it is a hopeful sign. The advent of healthy convic-
tions of any sort in Tas. politics would mark an advance
almost amounting to revolution. If the large red hand of
Fatman, as displayed in the wealth tax, is seriously
grasped by the Dobson-Lewis combination, and the Minis-
try makes a similar departure from tradition by sticking
to its present programme, the latter should score at the
polls, despite the Anchor trouble and the clatter the reli-
gionists are likely to make over the toleration displayed
by the Braddon-Fysh Government towards the wicked
Tattersall.
A section of the Victorian press has risen
to demand that the public shall not make
such a fuss about Baxter, and Rapiport, the
J’s.P. whom the Former Board found guilty of
corruption, on the ground that there are many
others quite as bad as they. “ A great show of
virtuous indignation,” says one paper, “has
been made about Baxter and Rapiport, as if
they alone were blameworthy.” In one sense,
of course, the show of indignation is rather
ridiculous; i.e., if it stops at Baxter and
Rapiport it will be ridiculous. But when it is
implied that these two should receive more con-
sideration than they otherwise would because
others who may or may not be equally guilty
have not yet been formally arraigned, the
argument breaks down badly. If the man who
is found out is to receive special consideration
because others have not been formally found
out, then Deeming should have been let off
because other murderers are still at large, and
every criminal should escape because other
criminals of the same sort have evaded dis-
covery. In fact, nobody should be punished
except the very last offender of all, and he
would have to be let off because the others had
been; and the suppression of crime would
never begin at all. Nobody ever applies this
process of reasoning to burglars, garrotters,
sneak-thieves, pickpockets and the like ; even
though a hundred burglars escape capture the
law comes down on the hundred-and-first
without the slightest consideration. And if
there are 100 unjust J’sP. in Victoria, which
seems quite probable, and the law has really
found two of them, that is a good reason why it
should do all it knows to find the other 98, but
for the two it is no apology or excuse whatever.
What is really wanted by the people who take
up this line of argument is that the law should
forget the two men it has definitely on hand in
an aimless hustle after other men whose names
and addresses are not given, and against whom
there seems to be at present no legal evidence
—in other words, it is invited to drop the bone
by wild cries that the shadow is running away.
Crick and M'Millan are substantially at one
on the glaring ineptitude of maintaining the
N.S.W. Postmaster-Generalship in its present
state. In no sense of the word is it, or should
it be, a political office, and the way in whicli it
has usually been filled proves that Ministries
generally look on it as a gift to some special
industry or clique or organisation, in expecta-
tion of receiving an amount of covertly-exercised
political influence in return. It is in turns
presented to a water party and a liquor party,
an Orange party and a Green party, a stolid
neutral party and an aggressive Labor party,
and the individual selected, admittedly without
any conception of the character of his duties or
any special ability to discharge them if he knew
what they were, quietly goes on pocketing
,£l5OO a year while his Government hangs
together. The billet in Australia is a repro-
duction of the English system, and is surrounded
with most of the evils which adhere to the office
there It extends the area of a Premier’s
patronage ; it puts in his hands a means of
stopping the mouths of malcontents and exciting
the gratitude of time-servers ; while all along
the taxpayers have to bleed for the perform*
anee.
Dear Bulletin, —Some Australian Judges are
rapidly building up a reputation for utterly
purposeless cruelty. In addition to two alto-
gether illogical sentences inflicted recently by
Judge Hodges, Judge Hood, at April Criminal
Sessions, ladled cut terms of solitary confine-
ment with a liberal hand. To one man he
awarded two years’ hard labor, and to three
others eighteen months, in addition to which
each and every sentence included one week’s
solitary in every alternate month right up to
the end of the sentence —to the eighteenth
month in the case of the two years’ sentence.
Possibly Judge Hood is not aware of the full
effect of this merciless punishment. It means
being locked in a cell 15ft. by 7ft. by 12ft. high,
from which all furniture save a stool has been
removed, from Monday morning at 6 o’clock till
the following Monday, excepting one hour per
day for exercise (also solitary). During the
whole of this time the prisoner gets only lib. of
brown bread per day—let anyone weigh out a
pound of damp brown bread and see what
a handful it is—and water. He is only
allowed the Bible and one religious work, pro-
bably a Sunday at Home —no other book what-
ever ; and —refinement of cruelty ! —he is not
allowed to work, even to pick oakum. In
addition to this, every three days of solitary
lengthens his sentence (by decreasing the
remission) by one day, as he can earn no marks
for work. I would stake my immortal soul
that one week’s “solitary” would make most
Victorian Judges drivelling idiots—or religious
maniacs ; and when one considers the physical
effects of seven days’ semi-starvation .on an
already weakened frame at the beginning of
every other month, it is safe to say that Judge
Hood has by this action shortened this man’s
life by years, in addition to further breaking-up
his already none-too-sound mental powers.
Truly when the English hung men for sheep-
stealing they were more merciful than Judge
Hood in thus punishing men for larceny.—
Victorian Justice.
Dear Bulletin,— “ Shark ” pleads for the
adoption of the English Judicature Acts, on
the ground that some of the law’s delays would
thereby be removed. No such thing would or
could happen. Practitioners know well that it
takes but a comparatively short time to arrive
at the ready-for-hearing stage, but after that
the chances are strong that the present Judges
would not be able to complete their work in
their allotted time—then over goes the case for
weeks or months. It would be as wise to lay
the blame on the coal when an engine does not
work beyond its full capacity. Again, the old
system is more concise, and just as free from
technicalities. For example : “ I deny the
allegations contained in paragraph 8,” does
not, under this wonderfully modern system,
deny such allegations at all. The pleader must
deny them specifically. It’s a case of the lesser
evil, after all. — Another Man-Eater.
The Sunday section of N.S.W. Licensing law,
although so generally defied, is right into the
hands of the informer and police-tout. The
other day a man was fined ss. for being found
in the act of leaving a Cook’s River (Sydney)
hotel with liquor in his possession. And the
hotelkeeper was fined £2. Anyone can see how
easily a hotelkeeper may be victimised under
such a law. A man may, under any of a thou-
sand pretexts, make his way into a hotel and
emerge with a flask, carried about with him for
the purpose. This, in fact, happened in Eng-
land, until the law there was repealed in t his
respect, and now a person must actually be
found drinking on the premises or with drink be-
fore him. In a late case a bottle was found on a
man, and a constable swore he saw the man go in,
and a sergeant and constable swore they saw him
come out of a gin-palace, but the court decided
that these facts did not constitute “finding”
him on the premises, and so there was no case.
All residential hotels are open on Sundays, the
public bar alone excepted, and as everyone may
walk over the premises, what is to prevent in-
former or perjurer, with a flask in his pocket,
from putting the landlord under the grip of the
police ? A first-class hotel has generally a
means of defying persecution like this, but
small, one-horse places in back streets have no
means. And the record of the cases which
reach the N.S.W. courts shows that 99 out of
100 are concerned with the one-horse pubs.

“Trilby” is getting along the same as
reported last week, and the gruesomeness of
Svengali grows more and more upon the
kind of spectator who loves artistic gruesome-
ness. There is a crawlsome fascination about
that man of grime and melody, and people
go home to dream about him, and then come
back to see him again and to dream some
more. And in their dreams Svengali wanders
oyer them throughout the silent watches of the
night after the manner of an alligator with un-
clean dishcloths on his feet, and the memory of
his grimy paws as they dance over the keys of the
piano suggests a family of serpents bounding on the
washstand. Trilby’s feet, however, have not grown
upon this paper since the last report appeared.
They are still the same pair of good, ordinary,
wholesome, every-day feet that they were before,
and it doesn’t believe that they will ever look like
anything else. Also, the pictured foot on the
wall—which was apparently done by Trilby stand-
ing on a chair on top of a table while her under-
standing was outlined by Billee —is nothing
especially astonishing. Trilby is a remarkably
nice girl, taking her all round, but a too-careful
analysis of the lady doesn’t yield such good results
as might be expected—she needs to be taken in
the “ altogether,” and taken that way she is quite
satisfactory. Last week The Bulletin objected
to Little Billee in a general sort of way, and now,
after seven days spent in inspecting and boiling
down its feelings with regard to that unsatisfac-
tory character, it believes the trouble is mainly
that he looks too old and hard and wrinkled for
the part. It would take a lot of ability to prevent
Bill looking sketchy and weak, for the part itself
has just about nothing and a half in it; anyhow the
whole reason of Bill is that he should be a nice,
enthusiastic boy unspoiled by drink or late hours
or bad tobacco, or knowledge of the Flossie that
leadeth to destruction, and the present individual
isn’t. A lady ought to have been put on the
job. One thing is certain —“ Trilby ”is not quite
the f urore in Sydney which this paper expected.
Probably the people who like to be fascinated by
the high art of Reuben Fax, and to dream of an
unbathed spectre crawling over them in the night,
are only sufficient to fill half the Lyceum, and the
other half is reserved for the people who want to
fail in love with Trilby’s feet, and part of this
second crowd is either disappointed with the feet,
or doesn’t see them long enough—they are only on
view about seven minutes, or it wants the calves
as well, and so stays away. The show is doing
well in a plain, ordinary way, but it was expected
to bulge out the universe, and unfortunately it
isn’t bulging any universe.
** * *
Mahomet, the talking horse, has again
placed himself on the bill at Fitzgerald Bros.’
circus, and business is all that any horse can
desire. Mahomet was more or less off the bill for
some days, having briefly intimated his intention
of taking a rest, and when he does this it is not
advisable to press him too hard, else he might
send in his resignation on the spot, and give up
circus business, and take to lecturing. One evi-
dence of the interest which Australia takes in
everything connected with horse lies in the fact
that the front gate of the circus opens now at 6.30
—performance beginning at 8. And people
actually rush in at 6.30, and look at the monkeys
with one eye for ninety minutes, while the other
eye is fixed on the seats to make dead sure that
they won’t be crowded out and not get a good
view of Mahomet, after all. And when a full-
grown citizen has got into his 75th minute of con-
tinuous looking at the monkeys, he is a saddening
spectacle.
** * *
Tennyson and O’Gorman have been added
to the cast at Sydney Tivoli, and, with the aid of
Charles Godfrey and the other people, they put in
a moist and miserable holiday-season with good
results.
** * *
“The Derby Winner,” which the writer
suspected on insufficient grounds of intending to
appear at Sydney Royal last week, is definitely
announced for Saturday. This estimable animal
is also quite new to Australia, and, his opponents
being palpably stiff, he is going to win the classic
event each night for the ensuing three weeks or so.
** # *
On Saturday Pinero’s ‘ ‘ Benefit of the
Doubt ” is to be staged at Sydney Criterion, being
its first production in the land of the wattle-poem
and the drought. Meanwhile, the “New Woman”
has been doing well, though not by any means so
well as that bright and festive outbreak deserved.
The last month of the B. and B. co’s last season is
now on, and an advt., which reads like the epitaph
of our best suicided friend, states that four weeks
hence the co. will be disbanded. This circum-
stance certainly ought to make Sydney roll up as
one man to drop the beautiful tear of sentiment
in the vestibule and up the red-carpeted stairs,
but somehow it doesn’t roll up any more than
usual. Presumably the beautiful tear of senti-
ment is off.
** * *
That very old drama, “Across the Con-
tinent,” which died out of the memory of Sydney
playgoers some 15 years ago, and is now about as
new again as if it was only written yesterday, was
dug out from beneath its dusty pyramid and
staged this week at Her Majesty’s. Said aged
production is so exactly like the drama of to-day
in its disconnectedness and its cheerful disregard
of law and probability, that it is evident the art of
writing the big dramatic horror hasn't advanced
much of late years. The boss Indian addresses
his accomplices at great length in a loud shriek
right up against the front door of the palefaces
they are going to surprise, and then tells them at
the top of his brassy voice to be silent and cautious
—just as he has kept on doing ever since. The
bad man is gaoled on the bare assertion of the
good man that it was he who did it—what he did
isn’t even explained. The drama contains
about the most realistic express train ever
seen on the stage, and a most desper-
ately unrealistic Indian scrimmage, in the
course of which all the parties tow each other
round by the hair. The rest is partly infuriated
but superfluous organ - grinder, nigger - minstrel
show, and public-house murder; partly a faint
recollection of a woman who was fatally thrown
down a stair 18 years before by a callous publican,
and whose husband died in the horrors ; partly a
story about a person who was convicted on false
evidence before the drama opened, and who tracks
his accuser with dreadful patience for years and
years, till at last he finds him and shakes him a
good deal in a public-house ; and partly a young
man who forged a cheque previous to the beginning
of the story, and whose position is explained by
the wicked character talking to himself after the
sandwich - man is gone. Alfred Woods has a
good deal to do with the unrolling of this
tale of woe ; so have Messrs. Hodson, Booth-
man, Ryan, and Hosea Easton ; likewise Misses
Lilia Wilde, Edith Barrow, and Mrs. Marshall.
These and the locomotive are about all the people
whose connection with it can bear inspection. A
lot of time is taken up by a prologue, which shows
the heroine’s father, whom nobody cares about,
dying of drink 18 years previously; and the vil-
lain’s father, who never turns up again in the
drama, playing cards with two men who are no
more heard of; and two other people, who
straightway drop right out of the narrative,
bringing victuals to the heroine’s mother who
isn’t mentioned any more after that. This sort of
irrelevance is painful.
** * *
Pastor Haskett Smith has been telling his
stories of Syria and Palestine, and the other
lands of dirt and glitter and old romance which
lie about the eastern end of the Great Sea, to
food audiences at Sydney Y.M.C.A. TTa.ll. The
■ulletin loves Parson Smith in some of his
aspects. He casts a pleasant glamor over the
camel-smelling cities and dog-and-fly-infested
towns, and the thirsty, dreamy wilds, of that land
where nothing seems to be real save the tax-
gatherer and the atrocity. He knows the over-
laden Oriental ass and the city mongrel by heart,
and he is acquainted with the crooked streets and
the water-carrier and the alleged Fatima, and he
has cultivated the dervish, and, in a general sort
of way, the place has soaked into him. And
anybody who is thoroughly acquainted with
anything is so very rare that it is no small
pleasure to meet an intelligent and intelligible
person with something really soaked into him.
It is only needful to steep Parson Smith in warm
water for about 30 minutes, and then take him out
and put him aside to dry, and large quantities of
information about the East will be found in the
tub from which he has been extricated.
** * *
“ Silver King,” as produced by the enter-
prising Westmacott, at Melbourne Royal, exceeds
expectation by several inches. This is a better
all-round rendering of the old story than some
that have gone before. Also, the show is bright
with new scenery, and radiant with financial suc-
cess, and boomsome in spirit and in truth. Bul-
letin belief in the comparative greatness of
“ Silver King ” remains. No work of its class has
rivalled the literary melodrama which Jones and
Herman sprang upon a weary world 14 years ago.
Other clever collaborators tried to do the same sort
of thing in the same happy way, but never quite
managed to get there, and melodrama, after
awhile, relapsed into evil ways under the iron rule
of the stage-carpenter. One finds no living
bridges, thunderbolts, conflagrations, horse-leaps,
or dynamite explosions in “Silver King.” An
awful railway calamity is reported in the evening
paper, but the accident doesn’t obtrude itself upon
public notice in the form of a tableau. The most
ingenious sensational incident on record was
deliberately shut out from view by the authors,
and has never been missed. Their story is the
more interesting for not being decorated with a
picture of its all-important event.
* * * *
Scot Inglis makes a very likeable Denver
all the time; he doesn’t whine in his cups, or
preach in his penitence, and, although he might
put a little more force into his discovery of the
dead body and subsequent flight from the police,
he comes through at the finish with honors thick
upon him. Miss Harrie Ireland likewise puts
a pleasing performance to her credit, and Elton’s
Jaikes is a valuable addition to the company. In
some respects Elton outshines Royce and Phil.
Day, who were older and droller Jaikeses than he.
Certainly his present treatment of the character is
more hearty than his original attempt, which
seems, by the way, to have faded out of recollec-
tion. Plimmer, as the Spider, errs on the side of
quiet naturalism, and Hazlitt, as housebreaker
Cripps, shouts too gently. J. W. Sweenev
(Father Christmas), Hans Phillips, Miss Gill, and
little Daisy Ireland shine forth, and among the
minor character-players, most of whom are very
good, Miss Reynolds (as Tabitha) proves particu-
larly useful in her amorous designs upon the aged
Dan’l. “ Silver King ” will prosper until furtner
notice.
** * *
The Alexandra bill for this week is a well-
meaning drama of bucolic disposition, called “On
the Wrong Track.”, Its human interest seems to
spring from a mysterious mixing of babies, and
when the infants are unravelled the entertainment
comes to an impressive close, the villain having
previously fallen with complete success from a
house-top. Augustus Glover, pretty Miss Blair,
and Wilson Forbes are satisfactory features in a
loiig cast. The trouble is mounted with consider-
able attention to small details, and a wholesome
smell Qf farmyard and arable land is expressed by
the leather leggings of several male performers.
*# * *
The direful failure of “ Miss Decima,” at
Melbourne Princess’s, was followed on Saturday
by a revival of “In Town,” which apparently
gave full satisfaction to a full house. “In Town ”
is in many places a comic opera rather than a
musical comedy, and possibly the new people
make it go as far as the “Gaiety Girl ” company
did a year ago. Comparisons between two different
ways of telling the same joke must always be a
matterof opinion. The writer prefers the otherway,
but without denying that the new performance is
harmonious. Miss Juliet Wray is quite as much like
an ex-governess in a duke’s family as George Lauri
resembles the typical captain who lives on his wits
and smart appearance. And Miss Florence Young
might be taken for a young lordling by anyone
who could accept Miss Nina Osborne as a Maud
Montressor. There is only one more week for the
Comic Opera Co. to stay at the Princess’s (on June
6th they sail for Maoriland —76 souls in all), and
“ In Town ” may last until the arrival of Mrs.
Brown-Potter and Bellew.
** * #
The first day of the Theatrical Carnival
at Melbourne Exhibition Building was not
brilliantly festive. Seventy distinct bicycle ex-
hibits, when they come to be viewed in cold blood,
are more entertaining than one bike, and the con-
templation of one blessed Nimrod-Zimmy in a
quiescent state grows monotonous after a time.
This year’s Carnival was not, of course, given over
entirely to bicycle, nevertheless it seemed to be
unduly weighted with pneumatic tires and patent
gears. Worse than that, the opening day was
damp and dreary, and the influential citizens who
had formerly come along with several shillings to
spend in sweet nonsense, didn’t roll up freely on
this occasion. The Bulletin hopes they arrived
on Monday afternoon and evening. There were
humorous and pathetic circumstances to be found
here and there. Marie Luella, standing behind a
circular counter, confronted by a tract of desola-
tion and smiling mechanically at nobody at all,
was a sorrowful sight. At another counter the
announcement, “Juliet Wray,” was painted on a
vast piece of calico, beneath which Juliet’s sister
figured in lieu of her relative, with the result that
the public in general said she wasn’t “as thin as
The Bulletin made out. ” Another funny mis-
take happened in the space reserved for the Opera
House company. La Belle Maie had charge of
an American gambling apparatus, which she
was working for a time on an unprofitable
system. Money could be staked on either of six
numbers, and any investor was bound to win if he
backed every number for the same amount—or so
the story went around. Anyway, the little lady
who had undertaken to run this novel concern in
the cause of charity, was observed to glance at it
with growing suspicion every time she set the
wheel in motion, and found herself a loser when
she settled up.

“ I was taking it easy and comfortable at the Oriental
Hotel (Melb.) without my corsets (with the accent on the
sets) when they rung me up on the telephone and wanted
to know if I could go to the Alexandra Theatre at once.
I knew that was engaged there in a subordinate
capacity, and I thought ‘ Heavenly Father ! he’s met with
an accident, and I’m the only American he knows.’ I got
inside my corsets and a hansom, and drove round to the
Alexandra, only to find , whom I expected to see
in fragments, waiting on the curbstone. ‘ Madame

•like to see an Australian rehearsal of “Uncle Tom’s
Cabin.” He’ ‘ Well, I’m ! Is this why I had
to put on my corsets ? ’ ”
The hat-kicking business in the studio-dance scene of
“ Trilby ” was interpolated, it is said, at manager William-
son’s suggestion. They might as well work up the inci-
dent into a contest, and have the bride-elect put a comic
crown, or something, on the head of the highest kicker.
Haddon Chambers is hard at work again, Sir Blarney
Tomato having gone to South Africa, says The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. His next play will probably be
seen at the Adelphi, but it is unsafe to prophecy any-
thing that the Gattis will do. They usually keep their
own counsel well.
Sturdy Harwood is in evidence again. He first trod the
boards in Sydney, with the tuition of G. V. Brooke.
Lawrence’s memoir of the tragedian includes a letter from
Brooke to Heir, his Melb. manager, warning him that
salaries must be cut down, “ except Lambert and Har-
wood. ’ But alas for the prudent ant that won’t act like
the grasshopper! Here is Harwood toiling at the oar,
and Dick Stewart in harness too as treasurer of the Melb
Princess’s.
“ M iss Decima” did not catch on in Melbourne. How
could it? The piece pivoted on Juliet Wray’s deter-
mination not to marry anyone but a young man who had
accidentally seen her legs. This was taking public
credulity too much. Is it conceivable that any young
man would want to marry the thin young person after
seeing those legs ?
Juliet Wray’s engagement has still some time to run
and the story that she goes to England in May is founda-
tionless.

The property man of a theatrical co., visiting Cool-
gardie, went to a broker to get some used-up mining-
scrip to do duty as stage bank-notes. “ All the scrip in
that safe there you can have for carrying, and the rest I’ll
pay you to take away,” said the broker.
Fax omits Beerbohm-Tree’s greatest scene. It is where
Svengali, feeling his magnetism on the ebb, kneels and
prays, a situation evidently prompted by the King in
“ Hamlet.” But a Jewish paper says the kneel is out of
character.
Ada Crossley, although invariably praised for her sing-
ing by the Hinglish press, is not yet a great popular
attraction. She hasn’t, thus far, been able to identify her
name with any new favorite songs, and, until she can do
that, the Australian contralto will not command “star”
prices.
Phil. Greenwood, who went ’Ome with the Eleven as a
cricket-reporter, also on theatrical business for W. andM.,
took with him a smart comic opera by two Melbourne
men, which may possibly get a show in London. Bar-
rister O’Hara Wood did the libretto, and Nesbitt, the Ask
musical critic, furnished the score.
The cast playing “Charley’s Aunt,” now in its fourth
year at London Globe, are sick to death of Brandon
Thomas’s farcical comedy. Their “lines” have lost
nearly all meaning for them, and are mumbled and muti-
lated out of recognition. Yet the piece draws and pays
well. The agreement with the owners states that it is not
to be withdrawn until the house is losing money.
Westmacott’s production of “ Silver K<ng” at Melb.
Royal, may fairly claim to be the worthiest show, all
things considered, ever staged in Australia at popular
prices.
Re Sappho Smith’s assumption that the name of Trader
(Zou-Zou) is unmistakeably Yankee, a Mr. Trader, of Bal-
larat, claims that his English great-grandfather founded
the Trader family by dropping two letters from his
original name of Threader. Several of his paternal
ancestor’s sons went to America, and “ Geo. A. Trader is
a branch of the same family,” writes a Ballarat cor-
respondent. However, seeing that Zou-Zou first came to
light in the North of England, he can’t be an American
branch of any family. Also, if the first English Trader
had formerly been a Threader, whilst the first ’Murkan
Traders never owned a different patronymic, the name
seems to be more Yankee than otherwise. And if it isn’t
—no matter. It has an unmistakably Yankee appearance,
anyhow.
Florence, the younger sister of Kate, Ellen and Marion
Terry, is the first of the famous family to pass away.
Florence Terry had begun to be recognised as a clever
ingenue actress when, many years ago, she married a
wealthy London solicitor and retired from the stage.
May 25th, Oscar Wilde completed the first year of his
two years’ sojourn in gaol. The second year will doubt-
less be reduced to nine months, or so, by good behaviour.
Oscar is oppressively good nowadays.
Edison claims to have so improved Ront-
gen’s apparatus that he can make either muscular
tissue or the bony skeleton distinctly visible by
merely graduating the distance of the human sub-
ject from the fluorescent screen. And Tesla, an
even more magical electrician, can see the heart
beating. The days of the old diagnosis are
numbered.
