The Wild-Cat Column.
One of the matters which burst up Chaffey
Brothers, Limited, was evidently the London office. The
London management sold land and shares to the extent
of £'85,000, and borrowed £34,000, and “ blewed ” £40,000
in expenses while doing it. This sort of London manage-
ment was calculated to bring down almost any enter-
prise. The second issue of debentures, floated by the
London office, realised £14,900 and £10,155 of this went
in expenses, so that the co. only got £4745 and owed
£14,900. The biggest asset in the balance-sheet is “ Con-
cessions, £438,094,” which is practically the goodwill of a
hopelessly-bankrupt concern, so it may as well be dropped
out. The concessions represent the right of taking up
438,094 acres provided the co. can do the needful irriga-
tion works upon the land, and as the co. can’t do anything
of the kind this item is practically dead. The next biggest.
item is “ Freehold Land £290,980 ” (16,000 acres at
£l2 and 24,745 acres at £4 an acre). This land
isn’t worth anything like the price set against it
unless the present irrigation works can be kept up, and
extended, and it is very doubtful if they can be. More-
over, it isn’t worth anything unless it can be sold, and as
the co., in the height of its glory and prosperity, only
managed to sell a little over 21,000 acres in eight years,
and part of this area has since been forfeited, the pros-
pect of getting rid of any more is very dubious indeed.
Practically, these two assets (£729,074 in all) look about
equally hopeless. Of the uncalled capital (£239,000) about
£160,000 has vanished with Geo. Chaffey’s insolvency, and
some of the other holders look so very unpromising that
this asset may also be mostly dismissed from consideration.
Of such assets as there are, a large number are specifically
pawned to different institutions, and the rest are pawned
tn masse to the debenture-holders, and there are not
enough left free to pay the cost of keeping the big irriga-
tion pump going ten minutes.
The two London co’s which guaranteed payment
of the debentures have both burst. Among the alleged
assets of Chaffey Bros., Limited, are 3615 shares in the
Mildura Coffee Palace, but as these have a liability of
£1174 on them, they look more like a dead loss. The
Vic. mortgagees, including a number of banks and one or
two insurance societies, seem to have a pleasing prospect
before them, for the settlement can only keep afloat by
the payment of very heavy irrigation rates, and if they
foreclose they will have to pay these rates together with
all arrears of same, and comply with the cultivation con-
ditions, and otherwise keep moving. Altogether there
doesn’t seem to be much of a prospect in the Chaffey
enterprise for anybody.
* * * *
The S.M. Herald says the awful revelations in
the case of Chaffey Bros., Limited, are “ very unfortunate
for the cause of irrigation.” Echo arises and says :
“ Why ? ” Irrigation had nothing whatever to do with
the smash of the Mildura co. That dreadful concern
made something like 15 per cent, per annum on the money
actually invested in the works, and if the co. had been a
legitimate affair, decently managed and working on its
own capital, it would have paid magnificently. But when
a lot of the working capital cost 44 per cent, in office-
expenses alone to procure, and 5 per cent, interest and 3
per cent, discount in addition, and when the co. borrowed
money at 8 and 10 per cent, and paid 5 per cent, more to
the man who guaranteed repayment, making the rates 13
and 15—that had nothing whatever to do with irrigation.
The irrigation business was all right.
• • • •
The fight between the debenture-holders and
the Maoriland Government re the ownership of the Mid-
land Railway is getting nearly due. The Government is
running the little fragment of line, pending a definite
settlement, on behalf of the owners, whoever they may
be, and now that the co. is ruined, and its astounding
claim for damages is disposed of, the debenture-holders
apparently are the owners. Nobody is quite sure about
this, but it looks like it. The State must acquire the line
some day for several reasons. The railway must be finished
because the country needs it. The debenture-holders
can’t finish it, nor can they work it. The State can’t
always run a line which is its own at both ends and some-
body else’s in the middle. Therefore, either the State
must own the private line sooner or later, or else build
its own line alongside, and let the existing dislocated
fragment go to rust. The debenture-holders have a lien
—or think they have—of £825,000 on less than 80 miles of
railway, much of it unequipped, and if the State takes it
over at that price it will cost in cash alone over £lO,OOO
a mile, which is wholly absurd, seeing that the State lines
were built and equipped for £7703 per mile. The
£350,000 or so which Maoriland has already paid
in land would make the total nearly £15,000 a mile,
but that £350,000 was paid to the dead co., and has no
connection with the debenture-holders’ lien. The Govern-
ment, however, has one advantage. If the debenture-
holders won’t come to terms, it can work the line on their
account for a good many years yet, and present its bill for
the loss every half-year, and keep on doing so till they get
tired and make a reasonable offer, or till it is time to
seize the property by verdict of the Small Debts Court, in
payment of the accumulated losses. Meanwhile, the old
co., despite the decision of the arbitrator that it hadn’t a
leg to stand upon, is still raging over the length and
breadth of Bull-land about the “disgraceful swindle”
perpetrated upon it; and the debenture-holders say they
want their £825,000 in full, immediately ; and Premier
Seddon smiles and waits. It is an interesting situation.
* « » *
The Federal Building, Land and Investment
Society of Brisbane presents a report which shows it is
making some attempt to tackle its difficulties. A year
ago its properties stood in the balance-sheet at their old
boom-values and professed to represent £102,655. Then
the shareholders wisely decided to admit that the boom
was dead and the values mostly paper, and they wrote
this item down to £62,083 ; wrote down the capital from
£107,186 to £38,340 ; and added £BOOO (out of the written-
down capital) to reserve, making it £20,000, to provide
for further possible losses in the future. In addition to
these disasters, it appears that the total receipts for the
year just ended were £5248, and the expenditure was
£7845, and as the concern thus went to the bad by
£2597 it will probably want all the provision for
future losses it can make. So far as appears, the
whole revenue from £llB,OOO worth of investments
doesn’t even yield enough to pay interest on £50,000
of deposits, debentures, and bank overdraft, so either
the investments must be tolerably bad or the inter-
est on the borrowed money must be remarkably steep, or
both. The salaries, office expenses, &c., are all dead loss,
there being no revenue to meet them after interest is
paid. If the Federal was working solely on its own money
it could pay a 5 per cent. div. and have something left
even now, but the boom custom of borrowing vast sums
at (apparently) 7 per cent, and buying properties with
them that only yield (apparently) 2j per cent, when the
bad time oomes, has left it with no prospects w'orth men-
tioning.

Melbourne Grand Coffee Palace Co. reports a
profit of £126 for the year ended 31st March. About four
shillings per cent. , * *
The steady-going Bank of Adelaide shows a net
profit for the year ended 31st March of £31,225, compared
with £31,048 at March, ’95. A 7 per cent. div. uses up
£28,000 ; £3OOO is added to reserve, making it £152,0uu ,
and £225 is added to profit and loss, making the balance
£15,514. During the past year the little bank has boomed
greatly. Deposits have increased by £464,000 ; cash on
hand has improved by £74,000; and note circulation,
Govt, debentures, and other items have also increased.
The cash and State debentures total £760,000, which, for
so small an institution, is a remarkably solid line of de-
fence. The Bank of Adelaide is the only survivor of the
four S.A. banks of a very few years ago, and, judging from
appearances, it is one of the most solid financial institu-
tions left in this continent.
* * * *
In Melbourne, recently, three interesting usury
cases were heard in one day. In one case, the plaintiff, a
female money-lender, advanced £5O on a P N, interest £l5
for each month, or part of a month, until paid—something
like 360 per cent, per annum. Also, she advanced £2O at
the rate of £lO interest for 16 days, and the same for
every succeeding 16 days—something like 1150 per cent,
per annum. In another case, another money-lender re-
ceived £l2 a year, for four years, as interest on £l5, and,
after getting £4B interest, he proceeded to sue for the
principal. Evidently there is plenty of scope in Victoria
for a law fixing a maximum rate of interest—such a law
as prevails in all, or almost all, of the United States, and
as the Seddon Government promises in Maoriland.
* * * *
Permewan, Wright and Co., Limited, announce
that the net profits for 1895, after allowing £lOOO for
depreciation, were £2051, and as the 10per cent, dividend
absorbed £3750, the concern went 11699 to leeward dur-
ing the year. The yearning to pay 10 per cent, under all
circumstances is a very solemn thing indeed.
* * * *
Melbourne Storage Co., a concern with illo,ooo
capital, which makes a year’s profit of £29, publishes a
balance-sheet with at least one interesting item in it. One
of its assets is freehold property (less £52,556 mortgages),
£65,485. A footnote adds that this must be regarded as a
liability rather than an asset. Therefore, it would appear
that the value is simply a book-value, and that the pre-
sent worth of the property, over and above the mort-
gage, is nil. And yet the co. still inserts it as a large
asset. There is something refreshing about the idea of
issuing a balance-sheet with a footnote stating that some
of the principal figures must not be relied upon.
* * * *
The Tasmanian Permanent Executors and Trus-
tees Association plods on in its unprogressive little way.
This half-year it made £494, and after paying a little div.
at 6 per cent, per annum on its little capital of £lO,OOO it
was £194 to the good. Last half it made £297, and after
paying its little div. it was £3 to the bad. For the half
ended March, ’95, it made £356 and was £56 ahead of its
dividend ; at Sept., ’94, it made only £265, and after pay-
ing the same old dividend it was £35 on the wrong side.
After 8)4 years it has no reserve-fund, and never seems
likely to have any, and its whole accumulations only
reach t 573. Altogether, this is about the smallest, sad-
dest, least exciting way of doing the trustee business im-
aginable.

A recent demand for Melb. Met. Gas shares is
now accounted for by a sudden spring of 9d. per 1000 ft.
in the co.’s gas-rate. Another turn of the screw was
meditated, the excuse offered to the writhing consumer
being the coal-strike. Nobody has quite enough inno-
cence to suppose that the ring’s increase in the price of
coal justifies the Bd. rise in gas, but it serves at least to
give an air of decency to the situation. Gas-consumers
in general are aware that coal has gone up. If they were
as generally aware that gas : shares had hardened on the
fact of coal becoming dearer they might protest against
the system which enables Fat Man to wring extra profit
from' a public loss. The Gas Co. charges househo.ders
with the advance in its coal bill, plus shixty per shent, or
thereabouts.
* * * *
Melb. District Police-court decided last Friday
that electric-light co’s have no power to recover either for
electric-light supplied or rent of meters. The ground of
the decision was that it is necessary for such co’s to
obtain a special order in Council to authorise either con-
struction of lines or the supply of electric-light. The
Melb. City Council, which competes with private electric-
lighting enterprise, is specially authorised, but the pri-
vate limited co’s will either have to shut down for six
weeks or so until the order in Council is granted, or trust
to their customers’ good faith to pay up. Either alterna-
tive will mean a serious loss to both co’s, which have only
just emerged from the dead-horse stage.
* * * *
It is on the cards that, two of the biggest Aus-
tralian steamship companies will amalgamate during the
next few weeks.
* * * *
If the strike doesn’t kill Newcastle (N.S.W.)
trade, combustible coal seems ready to do the business.
The Brabloch reached ’Frisco April 7 with her cargo in
flames, and had to be scuttled in harbor ; and the Repub-
lic, bound for same port, took fire at sea on April 10 and
had to be abandoned—the crew being luckily saved by a
passing vessel. The percentage of Newcastle missing
ships is now so heavy that insurance is difficult to get on
any terms, and owners are beginning to fight shy of the
port.

Money must be tight in Melbourne when the
local Herald can publish this sort of stuff under the head
of cable news “ Left to the Heirs. A Handsome For-
tune. London, Friday Night. The will of the late Mr.
Thomas Harbottle has been lodged for probate. The per-
sonality is declared at £28,496.” A special correspondent
who goes forth in the night (especially a Friday night) to
wire tidings of a £28,000 probate can’t be moving in very
plutocratic circles.
The final scene in the farcical City of Melbourne
Bank enquiry was played last week, when assistant-
general-manager Robertson attended at the Insolvency
Court to sign his depositions, and answer a few more
questions. Robertson, having admitted that certain of
the bank’s transactions seemed rather extraordinary,
Judge Molesworth remarked : “ Apparently the directors
knew nothing of what was going on, but I take it you
must have known.” To which Robertson replied that the
bad accounts were not discovered till after manager
Longmuir’s death. Then the examination closed with a
snap. Apparently the directors knew nothing, and the
only man who knew anything is safely stowed away in the
silent tomb.

Baker’s Creek (N.S.W.) G.M. Co. was formed
in N0v.,’87— eight years and a-half ago—and since then
there have been crushed 65,075 tons of stone for 143,8520 z.
gold, of the value, at £3 10s. per oz., of £503,482. Of
this, £232,500 has been paid in dividends, or 465. 6d. per
share, there being 100,000 shares in the co. In addition,
the mine has been developed and equipped solely out of
itself The whole of the machinery and plant has been
paid for out of profits, as also has the purchase of the
Baker’s Creek North mine.

regret its failure to accept £60,000 cash for the show.

or 14dwt 3gr. per ton. All hands have been discharged
bv chairman Barton, who says that the low yield is simply

and poor sections of the reef can be so averaged as to go
anoz. to the ton, and he promises divs. within a few

far it looks like a case of—poor mine, sack the manager.

Silver Hill Junction S.M. Co. is the latest
Barrier float. Capital moderate —£13,750 in 55,000 shares,
of which vendors take 20,000, besides £lBOO, which they
say they have paid for the properties. What they get the
20,000 shares for in that case is not very clear. The pro-
perties to be acquired were only recently pegged out:
the leases, in fact, are not yet granted. They are north-
east of the Junction, occupying a range of low hills
running parallel to the Broken Hill line and terminating
in the Round Hill. One of the claims used to be the
Silver Hill, before that Copper Blow ; the name Junction
is attached to it now, no doubt, because if the Broken
Hill lode didn’t twist back in the Junction it would enter
these claims. Junction manager Joel Phillips, by the
way, gave this corrtinuation-of-the-Junction-lode theory
(upon which some people were asked to go into the new
venture) a nasty knock when he reported : —“ This js one
of the best properties outside the Broken Hill line of
lode.” In the prospectus the whole statement is under-
lined —the public had better cnty underline the words in
italics here. Not that the claims haven’t got ore. They
have ; but the bodies are small and will be found patchy.
Junction North is now in a position to send out
well on to 1000 tons of ore a week. So far no big ore
contracts have been entered into, though Harrold Bros,
have taken 2600 tons of first-grade stuff; Block 14 is
taking some of the lower-grade ore. The body as it
stands in the mine looks very fine : if only there was
without doubt a long run of it, what a mine it would be 1
The coal-miners’ strike caused one or two B.H.
dressing mills to run short time, the North among the
number — an occurrence which looks not very creditable
to somebody or other.
Junction is going to sink a deep shaft far west
on its lease with the object of striking the underlay at
something like 1000 ft. Wonder if the Junction people
will have the courage to publicly announce the result.
The B.H. Proprietary people have invariably been “sealed
oysters ” where their deep exploratory works are con-
cerned.
B.H. South is going to have the “buck ’’which
was intended for the North, hut which failed. Two things
will be relied on —the increase in concentrate-production
(and the nearer prospect of divs. in consequence), and the
improvement in the lode at 800 ft. At this level the South
has, at time of writing, a big body—a very big body, in
fact—of hard, fine-grained ore, high in zinc and lead, but
very poor in silver. Even from the upper workings the
concentrates bulk only about 7oz. silver per ton, and 2f
tons of ore goes to the ton of concentrates. The works
put up a record in the second week in May—so 6 tons
(assaving 68'1 per cent, lead, 7 - 3 oz. silver, 7'B per cent,
zinc"' from 1379 tons ore. On account of poverty in silver,
combined with faulty high percentage of zinc, South con-
centrates are not snapped up as the products of the
other/mines are.
Shipments of B.H. sulphides to America have
begun.
The Central —Ashcroft’s process ; the Junction
—the Camden syndicate’s (John Howell’s); the British
the Burnham syndicate’s. The Camden syndicate has
pretty well a controlling interest in the Junction now, and
a good many Australian shareholders are beginning to
feel uneasy about it.
Observe the terms of the Burnham syndicate’s
compact with the British : it is “ for the lease of the
(British) company’s furnaces at Broken Hill and the sale
of ore and concentrates up to 100,000 tons annually.”
Furnaces will, it is expected, start within four months.
But the Health Dept, will see that they don’t start with-
out fume-saving appliances.

at the meeting of the Great Boulder, of which he was
chairman. The fall of the Londonderry from the richest
surface gold-show on earth to one of the meanest, and
the rise of the Great Boulder from ss. to £lO a share, were
shocks too great even for North. Like most W.A. reefs,
he suddenly gave out.

“ Menzies, W.A. Alluvial workers to the north
of Niagara are reputed to have secured 40oz. in 30 minutes,
and altogether have obtained lOOoz.” And it might have
been added that, being in W.A., the chances are they
would not get as much again if they worked for 30 weeks.
The average W.A. alluvial-claim can be worked out in 30
hours.
* * * *
Kalgoorlie is rapidly establishing itself as the
only great goldfield of W.A. The Great Boulder has
crushed 7337 tons for 38,7260 z., Lake View 5418 tons for
15,0680 z., and both mines are looking better to-day than
at’any other period of their history. Other big mines are
opening up around them —Boulder Main Reef, Bank of
England, Perseverance, Ivanhoe, &c. But for these mines,
found in quite a distinct formation, the W.A. boom would
have faded away.
* » * *
At last! Brown Hill, Kalgoorlie, 96 tons for
4620 z. Have they been crushing nearly three months for
96 tons ? This is W.A. to a nicety. Fifty times since the
plant started, the public, per medium of Argus, Age,
S.M.H., D.T., and other papers, have been told that the
new dry process of amalgamation and cyanidation was
<< working splendidly,” and its success assured. Now
we have It that the plant worked 300 odd hours to put
through 96 tons. And that plant cost upwards of £20,000 !
° * * * *
Black Flag, Proprietary (W.A.)—“300 tons of
machinery representing 50 heads have been delivered,
and the balance, 200 tons for another 50 heads, will be
delivered later on.” It was sold for £30,000 cash, floated
for £600,000, and the writer has it from a mining-man
who was on it recently that it may prove an Bdwt. show.
$ * * *
The lowest depth mined in Maoriland is 900 ft.,
where the Kapanga shaft has struck ore said to assay
£25 a ton.
* * * *
The railway will soon be completed to Kal-
goorlie. Without a railway terminus, without a div.-
paying mine, and having to carry the evil-smelling repu-
tation of scores of wild-cats, Ooolgardie is in a pitiable
plight.
* * » *
The Hill End little battery at Broad Arrow,
W.A., and not the mine, has been shut down. The
McDonald Bros., who sold the mine to Dr. Simon for
£25,000, are now running five heads on the Excelsior, the
first crushing from which went soz. to the ton.
* * * ;• *
“The Golden Bar mine, Ooolgardie, is now
getting 10,000 gals, water every 24 hours.” Well, it is
some consolation to have even a water-mine in Cool-
gardie. The plant is worked by other mines, and the
mine itself worked for water. And is this to be the end
of the gorgeously-rich Golden Bar?
* » * »
Blackwood, a Vic. field 30 years ago famous for
its nuggets and marvellous yields, is feeling the miner’s
pick again. Prospects are tip-top for a thorough revival,
as the country carries numerous gold-bearing lodes easily
worked.
* * * »
A trio of promising Ballarat Plateau shows—
South Star Extended, New North Bonshaw, and Western
Plateau—are to be offered to Bull and Co. The S.S. Ex-
tended has had £5OOO spent on it, but the others still wear
a good face in a good position.
* * * •
Bendigo has accomplished an important work in
underground-connecting the Hustler’s Reserve No. 2 and
Fortuna Hustler’s, making it possible to walk under the
busiest part of Quartzopolis—from the Mall to the railway-
station. Driving the 1516 ft. necessary has put more heart
into the Hustler’s Reserve, giving it div. resources, and
disclosing several big runs of promising stone yet to
develop.
New Chum Railway, from its 2875 ft. reef, has
the “ cake ”of the year —8670 z. from 540 tons. Reef,
since cut, has widened from Ift. to 9ft. thick in the centre
and 12ft. at ihe top of the level, with an immense tonnage
opened up. Some of the stopes are 14ft. wide and 10ft.
high for over 200 ft. in length. Co. has struck a deeper
reef, and, to get at it, will sink the shaft over 3000 ft.
Altogether, the Railway is proving a strong stimulant to
Bendigo’s deep-level digging.
Neighbor Shenandoah is not doing too well.
Railway reef, as it goes north, gets smaller, pinching to
4in. or sin., hence Shenandoahs are looking sickish in the
market. On the other hand, the reef is making strong to
the south, so the Eureka Extended has a good prospect of
getting it.
On the Hustlers line, the United Hustlers and
Redan still reigns the “ daddy” with 3oz. stone from the
900 ft. level. Hustlers’ reef has picked up somewhat, a
recent milling from a 6ft. reef below the 1200 ft. level
making the ounce. Hustlers No. 1, which many Sydney
people are backing, looks on the improve, but it has been
looking that way for the past six months without making
a decisive splash.
New Chum, pioneer of the British co’s, has a
payable reef at 550 ft. to cheer its oversea crowd. When
the mine is more developed regular millings will happen.
* * •
Minister for Mines Foster sanguinely predicts
Vic.’s ’96 yield at 800,000 oz. In a short time, he crows,
there will be 50,000 miners at work.
* * * *
Owner of the Pitfield Plains Estate, Ballarat
(Vic.), scoops in £2OO a month from ten tribute alluvial
parties. Royalty averages 5 p.c. Easy-chair mining is
the best pot.
* * *
South German, Maldon, is waking up Vic.
people to its worth. Last month’s milling, 1000 tons and
pyrites for 11560 z. 3dwt., div’d 2s. 6d. ; total distributed,
£102,000, equal to £4 ss. on each (paid to 9/6) share. Three
years’ milling stone in sight, and the co. is getting down
another 200 ft. so as to thoroughly prospect the south end
of the mine.
« * * •
Long Tunnel Extended, Vic., 1090 tons, 2118 oz.
—the highest monthly yield in its history. It has paid a
million in divs. Under existing conditions in Coolgardie,
a mine like the Long Tunnel would barely pay to work.
When J. Bull fully realises this little item, and finds out
what a hopeless show there is of the existing conditions
being removed for some years, a much longer time will
elapse before he will pour millions of money into a water-
less, timberless country where £5 worth of Long Tunnel
Extended mining costs at least £l5.
* * * *
Frederick the Great mine, Bendigo, has had a
crushing from a reef 40ft. wide, going Bdwts. to the ton.
The English shareholders no doubt think it about time
they had some return.
* * * *
Philip Davies has been on Eidsvold, inspect-
ing the Perseverance line, St. John’s Creek, on behalf of
an English syndicate. When asked by an anxious share-
holder what he thought of the Creek, Davies cautiously
replied that it was a fine place for grass and water. The
properties under offer consist of the Perseverance P.C.,
which turned out a lot of gold a few years ago, the No. 1
East Perseverance, and the No. 2 East. The No. 1 East
has just cleared up 134 tons stone for 19dwts. 14grs. per
ton. This was the result of a month’s work, and left a
credit of £250, which is to be spent in sinking the shaft.
Eidsvold is looking a little brighter, and this quarter’s
output should nearly double that of last. The Lady
Augusta is still getting rich stone, and the owners regret
that they cannot shift it to Westralia for the benefit of J.
Bull.

dit that the Darcys’ Mount Morgan income equals £6OOO a
month.

The Mt. Lyell directors’ report shows that the
mine is opening out much better than foreshadowed by
Dr. Peters. Whether the improvement warrants the pre-
sent price of the shares largely hangs on the success of
the new system of treatment—pyritic smelting.
• * * •
One of the saddest of sad sights is the number
of alleged mining-men invading Australia “ with valet.”
Two Australian mining-men are about to work this valet
business for all it is worth in London shortly, but on more
economical and mutual principles. Each will act as valet
to the other, week and week about. While money may
be saved in this way, it is feared that they will knock the
valet business right out.

CAMEO
Hardly ever, even in N.S.W., has Parlia-
mentary procedure touched a lower level of
pitifulness than it did, the other night, upon the
1 arkes-family question. Ignominious closing of
the (Treat Parkesian Legend ! Half-a-century or
so of ministration to personal glorification and
the mighty interests of State,” with, at the end,
no more than a long list of destitute children and
an ignoble public discussion of the widow’s
domestic affairs that didn’t stop far short of the
dust-bin in the backyard or the slush-bucket under
the kitchen-table. But, all unconscious of the
abject squalor of the whole business, Copeland
asked, and Reid answered, and the House sat
waiting for the darning of the children’s socks and
the scrubbing of the front-door steps, and the
hoisting of the worn-out garments on the line, in
pathetic unconsciousness of the hugeness of the
satire upon itself as a convocation of alleged
legislators. The “dignity of Parliament” would
be a mighty joke, if it were not such a hideously
miserable lie !

Governor Brassey’s recent expatiation, in
the presence of his second wife, upon the virtues
of her predecessor, furnishes a striking exception to
the rule that it is hardly ever the twice-married
man who persists in obtruding Number One upon
the notice of Number Two. Is it a larger supply
of sentiment, or a blunter sense of the appropriate,

or simply a laudable desire to keep hubby from
getting swelled-head and outgrowing his boots,
that rendersso many women ready to prate about
the dear departed, and to hang up, over against
the husband-in-the-flesh, the portrait of the ditto-
in-the.-spirit, and to behave generally as if they
wouldn’t • haYe any very violent objection to the
bodily'co-existence of the pair ? Or— per contra—
would a man do likewise, if the second wife would
stand it ? Or, again, is this alleged conduct on
the part of the second wife only a legend of the
dreary comic papers, after all ? Say on ! ye sex-
problemists—and spare not!
* * * *
A Bulletin correspondent :
A condition of practical mutiny prevailed on H.M.S.
Orlando during that warship’s last cruise. In the
Service ’tis alleged that to her crew the Orlando
is “ a floating hell.” Of 2000 (about) “punishments” re-
corded against the men manning the Australian Squadron
during 12 months, 1200 (about) were for (alleged) offences
by Orlando men. But about the mutiny. It occurred
only a few weeks ago. To complete coaling within a
given time, all hands were ordered to work from 4 a.m.
till 11 p.m. The men loudly grumbled against this
slavery, but their protests were unavailing. Just on 8 p.m.
a brief “ spell-oh !” was granted, and the limb-tired coaleys
threw themselves down to rest—a rest so acceptable that
when the whistle was blown not a man rose. The mutiny
was absolute. Of course there was the devil to pay, the
upshot being a court-martial. The ringleaders were sen-
tenced to various terms of imprisonment, which they are
now serving. [Where? In Australian gaols?—Ed. B.]
Apropos, how many men have deserted from the Orlando
during the past four years ?
* * * »
A Ballarat parson has been accusing Lord
Brassey of Sabbath desecration by throwing bis
yacht, the Sunbeam, open to visitors on that
blessed day. God, he says, will yet punish H.E.
by running the boat on to a rock. Secretly, The
Bulletin believes, the Sabbatarians are not at all
pleased with the “ Loard ” Himself for working
His sunbeams on the Day of Rust.
* * * *
A Melb. daily has the pluck to sneer at
the evening-dress regulation of Brassey’s morning
levee. -No man is fit to bow to the Governor
unless he has a spike-tail coat, but the farthing-in-
the-pounders all have them. In the array of the -
presented are all the elite of the Insolvency Court.
* * * *
Gov. Brassey’s objection to perform the
opening ceremony in connection with the new
operating-theatre at Melb. Hospital is easily ex-
plained. A few days before he was invited he
fell off his bicycle and cut his nose, and since then
the very thought of surgery has made him ill.
Another thing is that the performance of petty
functions palpably bores H.E. He finds that
Silken-Bond speeches are not wanted, but only
flabby compliments accompanied by a cheque, and
he is tired of that business.
Hobart Wes-
leyans have just cele-
brated the diamond
anniversary of the
establishment of
their Sunday-school.
The most numerously
attended class in the
early days was in the
gaol. During 75
years there have been
many exchanges be-
tween the gaol and
the outside classes—
but this fact isn’t
mentioned in the re-
port.
* *
The other day a
woman was charged
at Sydney with hav-
ing no lawful means
of support. The pro-
secuting constable, in
response to a Bench
question, said she
was a woman of the
town and lived by
prostitution. The
magistrate thereupon
discharged her, re-
marking that prosti-
tution was no crime.
There seems no doubt
that the magistrate
Is correct in Ms state-
ment of the law. A
person making his
(or her) living by a
method not illegal
clearly has lawful
means of support.
Justice Foster once
upheld, on appeal,
the conviction of a
youth for not having
“lawful visible
means,” though it
was proved that he
existed by running a
street “ tote,” which
is not an offence at law. The general impression,
however, is that this decision will he overruled
whenever the point arises before the Full Court.
* * * *
Another travelled Australian :
George Ashton helped me to look for rooms in London
and we visited seven houses, not one of which had a bath-
room. Finally, we struck a house with a bath, which we
found held the coal supply. The Englishman’s mornino’
tub would disgrace Kalgoorlie—and that’s all the bath he
takes, when he is taking any. Most London hotels charge
Is. for a shower and 2s. for a warm bath. As a compensa-
tion, one can buy good whisky for tuppence a nobbier.

Among the multitude of begging letters
which find their way to a certain vice-regal post-
bag was one last week which refreshingly broke
new ground. It asked H.E. to pay the writer’s
election-expenses, and promised in return to crush
the power of the Labor-party in Parliament, and
so at last bring back prosperity to our beloved
country.”

A sample of toys landed in
Sydney from a late ’Frisco boat
consisted of miniature wooden
figures representing men. The
toy appears first as a grandly-
supercilious personage, with high
collar, ample white cuffs, and
abundance of heavy chains and
rings. The direction is ‘ ‘ Tap his
shoulder.” When you tap his
shoulder a rapid transformation
takes place. The toy wriggles and
turns itself inside-out, and instead
of the mightily-conceited indivi-
dual of a minute before there
stands before you a convict in
chains. This box of curios is
labelled “The Australian Banker,”
the morose genius of some Yankee
inventor having been spurred by
recent incidents in Australian
financial history. .
The Launceston Benevolent
Society, at its latest meeting, ac-
knowledged among other useful
donations a parcel of cigar-ends
from a Mr. Day. Whether they
were the good Samaritan’s own
cigar-ends, or fragments plucked
from the gutter, the chairman for-
got to mention. It is to be hoped
that the ends justified the means
taken for turning them to a chari-
table purpose.

The “gent-one ” suckling in N.S.W. is in
tribulation. During the last few years there has
been a steadily-decreasing percentage of passes at
the various exams, whicli mark his five years’
articles. I his is partly due to an increasing
difficulty m the examination papers, and partly
to a more rigorous system of marking. Nor is this
the only indication of the way in which the
Lawyer-Guild is closing up.” Sydney solicitors
e “ titl '; d tu have three articled clerks each.
But the best firms have practically adopted a
quasi-Malthusmn policy, and decline to increase
the lawyer population except for a prohibitive
premium.

UNDER-PROOF.
I see her photos, everywhere
Her great eyes filled with
pensive thought
Or with an inspiration rare
Or shooting little lights ’in
sport.
From scores of windows in
the street
One finds her fair madon-
na s face
With rosy smiles, jocosely
sweet,
Or nun-like, shaded and
discreet,
Rise up to glorify the place.
Her naiad’s form in soft
repose
Doth some strange, dreamy
spell emit,
Or, garbed as Daphne, how it
glows
With subtle, sweet sugges-
tions lit!
She’s seen reclined in rural
shades,
Or standing in a queenly
way,
As regal dames and tender
maids,
ith hair in classic bands or
braids,
Oi tossed in lovely disarray.
A tender sentiment did ply
Within my heart, stirred by
those eyes.
Alas ! I’ve been defrauded by
The instrument that never
lies.
For I have seen herself at
last,
And when she frowns or
when she laughs,
And when she sits in pensive
cast,
Or walks with conscious move-
ments past,
She parodies her photo-
graphs.
Melb.
SILAS SNELL.
MY OATH!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
A Sydney doctor recently refused to be sworn on a
police-court Bible. He stated that he had “ sanitary
reasons” for refusing.
The Bible seen in court, I ween,
Doth double duty bear :
The inside tells the way to hell,
The outside sends you there.
The Bible met in court should set
At rest polemic strife,
For it is plain it doth contain
The germ of after-life.
Have then a care ; if asked to swear,
Be something more than loth :
A general term for every germ
Is “My (police-court) oath.”

That beautiful machine, the Yost type-
writer is gaining a reputation commensurate with
its great value. In less than four years it has
been awarded ten gold medals ; among the type-
writers ordered by the London School Board for
instruction purposes, the Yost’s total exceeded the
combined aggregate of all other machines taken
by one third. The Yost has been adopted by the
English, French, and Colonial Govts. The
Sydney Lands Dept., the Electrical Dept., the
Public Library, Norton and Smith, Gordon and
Gotch, &c., &c., have recently adopted them.
The working of the Yost can be learned in ten
minutes, and one hour’s practice a day will enable
anyone to write as fast as with a pen. After that
the rate quickly increases with practice. The
Yost has no ribbon ; prints direct on the paper.
Free trials and tuition at ThVUnited Type Writer
and Supplies Co., 114 Pitt-street, Sydney and at
Melbourne, Brisbane and Adelaide.*

* * * *
In deciding to prosecute Rapiport and
Baxter, the cronk Melb. magistrates, Attornev-
Geperal Isaacs has simply given this precious pair
an opportunity of rehabilitating themselves and
coming out with expanded chests to pose as
martyrs. Doubtless the notorious beaks justified
the Lormer Board’s decision, but no one with any
knowledge of law, or of tribunals of justice, for
a moment thinks the evidence adduced before
the Board of Enquiry sufficient to gain a convic-
tion. The prosecution is to take place upon a case
in which the principal and, in fact, only material
witness, is a woman who wobblesomely alleges
that while a hotel licensee she bribed the accused
to adjudicate in her favor. As there is no direct
corroboration a conviction is utterly unlikely,
especially in view of the manner in which the
witness has backed and filled.

A Sydney firm of brewers has sent up a

Dear Bulletin, — You quote the remark
of a Sydney lady-medico—“ Our friends behave
badly when they found our claims to practice on
the demerits of medical men ” —as though there
wbre good grounds for it. But who has accused
the male practitioners of any more demerit than
naturally attaches to their sex in treating female
patients? We friends of the doctoress merely
contend that she is qualified to cure one-half of
humanity, whilst the doctor is the fitting person
to practise on the other half. In his proper
sphere the medical man has no demerits nor is
the assertion that he ought not to undertake the
medical woman’s duties any slur upon his charac-
ter. If it were the fair sex that had monopolised
the profession of physic we would ‘ ‘ barrack ” for
the introduction of doctors, even as now we sing
praises to the doctoress. Your Sydney lady-
medico is, I imagine, putting in a word for her
husband, present or future. She has married a
medical man, or hopes to, and would have it under-
stood that he is quite as meritorious a person as
herself. So he is. Hitherto he has been a bad
thing for sooiety, but the lady, under the same
circumstances, would have been no less unsatisfac-
tory.— E.E:

Dear Bulletin,- —Re-
cent conversation between
a paid collector for an Aus-
tralian Benevolent Asylum
and myself -Q.: Can old
people be put into these
charitable institutions by
relations who pay so much
a month? A.: No. Q. :
What if I could prove that
one well-to-do civil servant
pays .£1 a month for his
father? A. (after much
beating-about): Well, if he
gives it only as a dona-
tion. ... ! Q. : Say
two old people are put up
for a vacancy —one of them
has a relation who will give
=£l a month as a donation,
and the other has no
friends—how then ? A.
(after a long pause): Well,
I would report favorably
on behalf of the one that
could, pay, for I get my
commission out of it. Q. :
And what about the other
two unfortunates—they go
to gaol, I suppose ? A. :
Well, yes. Q. : What if,
after paying, say, for six
months, the relations
stopped payment ? A. :
Well, you see, that is awk-
ward ; we can’t turn the
old people out if they be-
ll ave themselves. Q. :
What does it cost to keep
an inmate? A. : Four
shillings per week. Q. :
Do you think this dona-
tion system is fair? A. :
Well, no ; but all the in-
stitutions are the same.
The paying people get the
preference. Q. : And the
others go to gaol ? A. :
That’s so.— Subscriber.

breach-of-promise case it transpired that the pay

only ,£7B per annum, and of a third officer but =£l2
more. On this a deluded girl expected her swam
to live and keep her in luxury ! All is not gold
that glitters.. Brass-buttons glitter.

his plate): “Airs. Smith, this is the second
time that dirty cook has allowed this to
happen.”
MRS. SMITH : “ Oh, it is not the cook's fault,
but the second cook’s. He has omitted to
strain the gravy.”

Broken Hillites are raising Cain because,
while business - people keep Adelaide, time, the
local Govt officials keep Sydney time. The chief
trouble is that this Sydney time makes the pubs,
close an hour earlier than if the police used Ade-
laide standard.

eight harmless lunatics in
Svdnev who wander va-

most deep-

iac of the
crowd is the
brother of a
now - forgot-
ten Q. poli-
tician. Eight
or ten years
ago he be-
came con-
vinced that
the N.S.W.
Govt, owed
him .£50,000,
and for months
he drifted into all
sorts of public
offices, and tried to
collect his money
from the Superinten-
dent of Explosives, or
the Chief-Justice, or the
Valuer under the Real Pro-
perty Act, with equal hope-
fulness and vim. At last a
venerable messenger, who repre-
sented himself as the Colonial
Treasurer and principal engine-driver
to the Neglected Children’s Department,
explained to him that the money had
been paid to his credit at a certain Sydney
bank and for about seven years he has called
there for it persistently. Sometimes he calls
three times in a week; at other times, once
in six weeks satisfies him. In wet weather he gener-
ally insinuates a dripping gamp into the ledger-
keeper’s department to draw that officer’s attention,
and hands up his cheque to be initialled ; or else
he runs after the general manager, if he happens
to be about, and tries to catch him with the crook
of the umbrella and explain things to him. The
result is mostly an entry in the petty cash, “ Gra-
tuity to lunatic, 3d.,” or something to that effect.
On two occasions he has managed to look sane
enough to persuade speculative lawyers to serve
writs on the institution, and once he filled in a
deposit-slip and handed it in along with a large
chunk of mud which he wanted to put to his credit
immediately.
On one blissful occasion he vanished for fom
months, and it transpired that the Queensland
politician had shipped his eccentric relative to
England. Then one day a cipher cable arrived
from the London office : “ Blank and Co., solici-
tors, suing behalf J. Thingumbob, =£50,000. Cable
instructions. ” It was the well-remembered lunatic
again, and it took a lot of expensive cablegrams to
settle the trouble. It was settled at last, however,
and then, three months later, just when the local
office was settling down to its accustomed calm, a
familiar moist umbrella appeared over the parti-
tion of the ledger-keeper’s department, and the
familiar cheque for =£50,000 was handed up to be
initialled once more. The office lunatic had come
back. Then the messenger, with great presence of
mind, rushed him into a cab, told him that a
beautiful female of immense wealth was waiting at
the Coffee Palace to marry him, and sent him off
at a gallop. There was a considerable row at a
number of coffee palaces that day through a
strange man trying to hustle the nearest waitress
into a cab and take her to the registrar there and
then, but fortunately no one was killed
* * * *
The other Saturday afternoon, prior to the
theatrical carnival, a few hot and agitated females
were observed to haunt a particular spot in the
main hall of Melb. Exhibition building. Bicycle
races were happening in the quadrangle outside,
and the haunted spot proved to be the riders’
dressing-room, or reserve, closed in with a large
strip of matting. A hole in this fragile partition
accounted for the feminine excitement. A peep
through the aperture revealed an array of naked
bikists, woefully thin and nubbly creatures in all
cases, but men, nevertheless. The peepers seemed
to take it in turns to satisfy themselves re the sex
question, and the effect of a prolonged enquiry
on their delicate organisations was thrilling to
witness. One damsel needed assisting away from
the spectacle, she was so overcome by shame, or
something. Besides, the other ladies were wait-
ing.
* * * *
At Sydney Central last week, after a
woman charged with stealing a purse from a
customer at Mark Eoy’s had given evidence, the
arresting constable put several questions to her,
“ What was she doing in Mark Eoy’s?” She re-
plied that she was buying some goods. “ What
did the goods consist of ? ” They consisted of two
bottles of medicine and some hosiery. Then, in
stern, solemn tones and with his fat forefinger
lifted in menace, constable McQuacker uttered
these memorable words: “Will you swear that
you didn’t tell me yesterday that it was medicine
and stockings you bought?”
. . . . Love me,
If one day only ; let thy sweet, red lips,
Redder than dew-moist red pomegranate buds,
■Drp'p gently on my brow with soft flower-touch.
Clasp me fast circled with your foam-white arms,
Dropp thy small head till all thy sun-bright hair
Floats cloudwise o’er my face.
Speak in love murmurs ; let thy stream-like voice
With tuneful ripplings flow about my heart,
And lqll me with the dream that thou art true ;
Then stab or leave—if it seem good to thee—
But one hour of thy false and most sweet love
w ere. worth the truest heart that ever brake for
man.
A. B.

Sydney, May 26, 1896.
Mr Dear Moorabinda—
The Hampden regime is breaking the Australian
record for fervid patronage of all sorts and condi-
tions of charities, beginning with lost dogs—
in dogs Her Excellency delights. And Gov.
Hampden’s natural gift of airily cheapening
everything his audience secretly but resentfully
worships makes a “ somewhat different ” vice-
regality of him. His artistic method of touching
on the tender subject of the aristocracy in a land
now politically run by the cocky Premier who
threatened to supply his own Gov. if the Authori-
ties didn’t look sharp about it, amounts to inspira-
tion. Gov. H.’s latest irreverent reference to his
own class occurred in his speech at the City
Mission meeting, when he averred that hereditary
peers and paupers, felons and lunatics had equal
voting power. To my mind His Ex.’s cavalier,
quizzical way of sizing up the nobility, is likely to
be resented by a Lord-fearing Society which daily
prays for grace to be able to take the Royal family
seriously, and doesn’t want any undermining in-
fluences within the camp. And His Ex. ’s oratory
also suggests that the utterances of the Talking
Classes at ’Ome is shaping itself more to suit
Agnes Ebbsmith than the Duke.
The perfervid manner m which Gov. Hampden
shoves along his spoke of the wheel of Charity
rather makes society hump its back. Governors
are not expected to push any show as if they had
shares in it. A smile, a donation, a graceful pose,
and a few ambiguous remarks are their part of the
bill, but Gov. H. rubs it in so that you expect to
see him get off the platform and go round with
the hat. At the aforesaid City Mission show,
H.E.’s enthusiastic appeal to Society to put its
hand in its pocket (if it can find it) took a lot of
people the wrong way. Women who go about
seeking the shy subscription for the in-every-way-
deserving City Mission, tell of the philanthropist
who so objects to having his right hand know
what his left is up to, that he always refuses to
put his name on their lists, and prefers to give his
charity indiscriminately—which is not at all.
According to Gov. H. (who talks too fluently on
all subjects to be very earnest in many of them),
“ indiscriminate alms-giving is a reprehensible in-
dulgence,” so anybody who aspires to Fashion is
herewith solemnly informed that it is not Guv-
mentouse form to relieve anybody except per
medium of a subscription-list which gets published
and lets everybody know how many sins the
charity of the upper ten is likely to cover.
Speaking for myself, I detest the cold, fashion-
able doctrine (preached also by no less a personage
than Primate Smith) of “ I never give money in
the street.” It is very English and most anti-
Australian, for, thank goodness, much of the
kindly, hospitable spirit of the Bush and the Old
Times still lingers in the breasts of dwellers in the
city, where men and women are so much more
wrapped up in their own affairs, and are so much
more familiar with the sight of absolute destitu-
tion than those who dwell under miner’s canvas or
in bush huts. I even go so far as to reverence the
simple superstition of the circus-man who told me
the other day that he never refused money to a
beggar. He dared not —it was unlucky, he said ;
and that man carries his belief to the extent of
walking far out of his way to get change, rather
than allow any man who asks him for a meal to go
unfed. To my mind, the sawdust man’s creed
that it is unlucky not to feed the hungry when
and where they ask for food forms one of the chief
elements of true religion.
The Hampdens paid a visit to Sydney Hospital
the other day. After inspecting the majority of
the non-catching patients, “ to each of whom Lady
Hampden addressed a friendly word ” (after all,
vice-regal amiability iB too catholic to be comfort-
ing—where is the social distinction of the thing
when everybody has the right to refer to “wot
Lady ’Ampden sez to me ” ?) and doing the dismal,
over-clean halls of suffering from kitchen to coffin,
the V.R. party left (so say the papers) “express-
ing pleasure at what they had seen.” Yet nobody
but a doctor or a morbid soul finds any real plea-
sure in the disinfectant-haunted atmosphere of a
hospital which sends most visitors away feeling
that Sudden Death has ’em by the baok hair.
Sydney University Society presented a serious
play in five acts at Sydney Gaiety Theatre last
Thursday, entitled “Fanchon the Cricket.” De-
spite clever Frau Buhrow’s sprightly and finished
acting in the title-rdle, Miss Marks’s excellent ren-
dering of the witch, Mr. Gregan M‘Mahon’s
wonderful make-up as the stern parent, and Mr.
A. B. Davies’s shapely grey legs as Landre, who
had that most awkward of amateur roles —the
amateur lover, who hasn’t the lady’s mamma’s or
husband’s permission to hug her in front of all these
people and has to struggle with his unconvincing
love as if the girl were standing within a rabbit-
proof fence—despite all these bright spots in a
tedious narrative, “Fanchon” was a frost.
It was five acts long ! The “ unflaggingness ”of
the star became too apparent, and even her partial
disrobement on the stage and the fact that the
morris-dancers, told not to wear their frocks too
long, erred decidedly in the opposite direction,
were of no avail; in “ Fanchon ” “ the duds ” get
bowled for a goose-egg for once in a way. Domes-
tic drama is not suited to amateurdom, since it is
written that whenever an amateur wants to make
you laugh you weep. The ’teur who departs from
light comedy or farce is, therefore, lost. A weird
feature of “ Fanchon ” was that people wore the
same clothes for five acts, with an interval, too,
where one year passed away and took its time
about it, judging by the way one felt. Nobody
changed frocks but the star, who made several
effective changes and must have been pleased to
see how clean her lover had kept his best jerkin
after breaking his heart in it for twelve months.
“Fanchon’’drew a fair-sized house headedbytwo
Y.R. damsels, the eldest in white silk “bent ” (as
we say in heraldry) with pink and blue, and her
sister in cream chine. The last Gov. to go to a
U.D.S. show was poor ex-Gov. Duff, and he, so
far as I can make out, cost the society two-pun-ten
and survived the ordeal 18 months. By the way,
the Hampdens are rare patronisers—the largeness
of their family circle enables them to shed a V.R.
halo over a number of engagements at once. Of
course, it is only a subdued sort of halo when the
girls head the procession, but I confess I like it
when there’s a V.R. damsel in the lead. She is very
punctual, in most cases, and there is, as a rule, no
“God Save.” The raciest feature of the show
was after the morris-dance, when the dancers
flocked into a stage-box in their ballet dresses,
floral-wreaths, and paint, against a background of
under-graduates in boiled shirts. One damsel was
using her opera-glass so that it looked like a
domino, and the whole scene was really Frenchy.
After the performance the company received a
good many of its friends on the stage.
The ball given by Commodore Hordern to
the members of the Prince Alfred Yacht Club and
their friends, at Paddington Town Hall last
Friday, was a distinct success. The hall was im-
posingly decorated with huge floral anchors, stars,
palms and ropes of greenery, and a fleet of model-
yachts apparently held a regatta on the platform.
Music and floor were excellent, and people who
“ dance the Minuetta but like the supper better,”
as they used to say in “Djin-Djin, must have
had a good time, seeing that the supper-room was
practically open all the evening. The huge crowd
of guests were received at the top of the staircase
by Mrs. Hordern in a pearly-color brocade with
lappels embroidered in gold sequins; one or two
crimson roses and a dazzling array of diamonds
making an effective finish to the handsomest dress
in the room. Mrs. H. Daniels (wife of the Town-
Clerk) wore an artistic dress of white silk pat-
terned with mauve irises, a berthe of white chiffon
with small bows of purple velvet, diamond orna-
ments. Mrs. John See was in pink brocade and
a deep cream lace collar ; Miss Dolly Hordern in
a debutante frock of white silk and valley lilies.
A novel feature of the ball was the number of
juveniles. Ever since the Governor’s youngest son
was seen whirling in an Eton suit at a Guvment-
ouse hop, the best-regulated nurseries have refused,
on the grounds of obsoleteness and bad form, to go
to bed at seven when their parents were giving a
ball.
If the craze for fitting Little Billee into an Eton
suit and starting him off on the beeswax continues
to spread, the dancing-man may find himself out
in the cold. I hear that at a suburban spinsters’
dance the other evening, the number of small
schoolboys successfully finding places on all pro-
grammes made many a grown-up youth do the
wall-flower act. The only reason that I can see
for this toleration of the unfinished is that the
dear girls are getting their hand (or should it be
foot?) in so as to be able to dance with foreign
naval-officers, who are usually little Johnnies who
look as if they had been grown under pots. At
the aforesaid spinsters’ dance, I am told, several of
the unplucked rosebuds were carrying on quite
h, la Clontarf, whistling, shouting, whirling till
they flopped, and generally making hay of the con-
ventionalities. And yet they wonder, I suppose,
why they are still spinsters.
The Fresh Air League ball, on the 3rd prox., is
going to be a great success, if the sale of tickets is
any criterion. The object of the “ Fal ” ball is to
prevent suffering by giving change and rest to
weary work-girls, so everybody cordially hopes the
Centennial Hall will not be large enough for the
attendance next Wednesday evening. On June
30th the ever-popular Tempe ball (the “men’s
ball,” though I really don’t know why) occurs in
the Town Hall; and on June 24th, the Randwick
dance, in aid of the R.C. Church, takes place in Pad-
dington Town Hall; while the French ball (where
nothing ever happens like the dancing in
“ Trilby”) will be held at the same place on the
15th prox. Two private dances, that look well on
paper, and may run into an inside-place, are the
Neutral Bay Tennis Club hop, at Need’s rooms, on
the sth June; and the Centennial Tennis Club
bread-and-butter dance, at Paddington Town Hall,
on the 25th. But I would like to rise and solemnly
ask the committees of the last two if they have got
an aide-de-camp to throw the light of his profile
on the proceedings ? If they have not, they are
lost, for we have it on the authority of a Sydney
Society scribess that, without one of these
“ coveted prizes,” no entertainment can hope to be
taken seriously.
The most depressing thing about the Sydney
Turf Club races on 25th inst. was the forlorn way
in which feminine society promenaded unescorted.
Even the vice-regal girls tailed off down the lawn
with two girl-pals who rescued them from lonely
grandeur in the midst of the Governor’s box. I
don’t think the “coveted prizes” quite acted as sich
at the races. Capt. Stanley, in a pink shirt-front,
did the most of the squiring—but I suppose even
prize aides-de-camp cannot accomplish the impos-
Bible. To provide a Jack for every Jill in the
Guvmentouse-party would be indeed a big order.
I don’t wonder that at least two very pretty
Society girls were left to promenade the grass in
one another’s society. Their attentions to the
navy are so marked and exclusive that when the
Fleet is cruelly ordered away to the nearest polo-
match it is only natural that civilians with many
dance-refusals rankling in their souls should seize
the occasion to return a Roland for an Oliver.
Gov. and Lady Hampden turned up (late) for
about five minutes at the races (the birthday of
their Sovereign means a lot of wear and tear to
her representatives). Her Ex. looked very well in
purple with a small waist, a couple of pink roses
in her bonnet supplying the inevitable violent
contrast; while His Ex. in a glossy top hat (he
has abandoned straw hats to the races) and pencil
umbrella (and other things, of course) was a soul-
stirring spectacle. One V.R. damsel wore a helio-
trope dress verging on the smart; another was in
brown with cream satin collars Puritan" shape-
just like a glorified edition of the frock the vir-
tuous heroine goes to work in while hubby is in
gaol in Bland Holt’s plays; and a third sported
ultramarine-blue cloth with reefer coat and black
velvet hat with black plumes and satin ribbon.
By the way, one hat in the V.R. party was in-
teresting—it had a plateful of fiery roses plastered
all over the lady’s back hair, as if her occiput was
a verandah-pole.
Dot Boucicault did the lawn with a smartly-
dressed society matron in biscuit, with biscuit-net
over the sleeves and edgings of brown fur, white
felt toque trimmed with violets; a,nd another
taking gown was a Parisian-made toilet of black
satin, fine anchovy-red cloth, and black jet, worn
by a legal man’s wife. The most peculiar turn-out
looked like something between Mrs. John Bull
and a costermonger. It was dark-blue with little
brass buttons in threes down the front, and the
hat was the exact shape J. Bull wears in the pier
tures. A tourist female turned up in a grey check
suit with a grey felt hat, man’s shape, skewered
on, however, with very feminine bonnet-pins ; and
I can’t say I liked the look of a girl with a turn-
back over her shoulders of crimson velvet edged
with fur—it looked too like the historical sheep-
skin garment of Bryan O’Lynn. The races, by
the way, were postponed from Saturday to Mon-
day, which may account for the stale look of most
of the frocks, and certainly explains the toughness
of the hind-legs of the poultry in the members'
luncheon-room.
The committee of St. Vincent’s Hospital ball
are crowing over their grand total, £441 4s.—the
second largest amount the ball has ever netted.
It is rather disheartening that the day (Saturday)
when the Village Fair at Sydney Exhibition (held
by the W.C.T.U. and the Y.M.C.A., and all the
other initials that signify cold tea, and opened by
Lady Hampden) trusted to make the biggest haul
in aid of the Cawse was (irony of appropriateness !)
the wateriest rainy day of the season. A thing
like that is like drowning a cat in cream, and
makes the godly seriously critical of the ways of
Providence, which ought to know better than to
send down the prohibitionists’ favorite liquor at
the wrong moment and spoil the sale of the ginger-
beer. It really looks as if Providence and Prohi-
bition weren’t quite so chummy as the latter tries
to make out.
A more or less recently-unpacked Australasian
Gov. (not N.S.W.’s) sets an example of thrift in
high places that fairly entitles him to the belt as
champion skinflint. A recent exploit of his was
to march into a leading ironmonger’s and ask to
see some filters ; having chosen one, he wanted to
know if he could procure it at Home price ? The
salesman apologetically hinted that freight, duty,
and profit precluded the possibility, upon which
H.E. calmly remarked, “very well; if you hear
of anybody having a good second-hand one to sell,
please send him to Govt. House.” Should this
sort of thing continue, the suspicion will gain
ground that Her Ex.’s millinery is procured
through the medium of London women-journals
in which “ lady shortly expecting to go into mourn-
ing will exchange Gaiety - girl’s turn-out for a
second-hand or misfit coffin.”
But society really cannot afford to look pained at
tales of V.R. economy. The self-made Society
person who has shaken hands, as it were, with every
£ in his banking-account is also careful to the
verge of eccentricity with his worldly wealth. For
example, the subscription-lists for the terrible
Brunner mine disaster, opened in Brisbane, showed
a total of £5O after three weeks’ begging, although
Maoriland generously poured in thousands when
the same city was in trouble over the floods of ’93.
Meanness is one of the good old Hinglish attri-
butes of the “ best ” people everywhere in Anglo-
Saxondom, and any Govt. House which thinks it
can give anybody a lesson in cheeseparing, or how
to skin a flea for its hide, is merely wasting its
time.
The friends of a red-headed and popular actress
at present on the sick-list, were encouraged to
believe, the other day, that all might yet be well
when the prepossessing invalid rose and demanded
a bottle of hair-lotion with which to anoint her
tresses. A most promising sign. The woman who
sends out for cosmetics is as good as convalescent.
By the way, what a lot of Titian-red hair there
is knocking about just now—ready for the fur
season, I suppose. “ Shall I tell you a secret ? All
women look well in fur,” says a lady in “Re-
bellious Susan,” but it is only partially true,
after all, for bright hair is a sine qua non. All
this red hair looks so picturesque that it’s almost a
pity Condy’s fluid is responsible for the bulk of it,
though it certainly looks more ingenious than
gold-wash, and one perceives in it an admirable
dodge for the red-headed man going grey to dye
his hair red. Nobody would ever suspect the
trick, which is one of the distinctest advantages
of being red headed.
I hear that a gilt-edged young Sydney lady,
who recently went to England accompanied
by a lady friend, to fulfil a matrimonial-engage-
ment, is returning to Australia, still unwed but
engaged to someone else—her travelling companion
having supplanted her in the effections of the man
she went ‘ ‘ home ” to marry.
Writes my Melb. correspondent:—
“ A quiet Melb. divorce-suit, that happened a
while ago, might have been mentioned at the time
as a warning to sympathetic couples. Mrs. Blank
was a rather aggressively proper person up to the
age of thirty, when, with her husband's approval,
she appeared in the role of “ court companion ” to
a lady respondent. The case in question lasted
many days, and its giddy details probably under-
mined the stern propriety of the matron who had
come along to keep her unfortunate friend in
countenance. Mrs. Blank slowly renounced the
authority of Mrs. Grundy and became an altered
woman. Finally she figured once more in the
court, this time with a friend to keep her in
countenance, and the decree nisi which had
formerly moved her to weep for another woman
now set the tears flowing for herself. The story
sounds like an argument in favor of ordering
females to leave the court when spicy evidence
falls due. Are the Social Purity Society ladies
listening ?
“ Miss Daisy Madden, the Chief Justice’s pretty
daughter, has returned from Europe with her own
special specimen of the fine family voice beautified
and strengthened by her course of lessons under
Marchesi. Miss Madden was a great favorite
with ‘ Madame,’ who has declared to others that
if the pretty Australian girl could take to the
stage, she would make a great reputation. The
Madden girls are all clever as well as pretty, and
their admiration for their much -moustached father
is only equalled by their affection for their still
beautiful mother.
“ Gov. Brassey is seriously engaged with a
Home Reading Union which holds its meetings at
Govt. House. Brassey’s speeches, Queen Vic’s
‘ Life in the Highlands,’ the Life of the Prince
Consort, and similar treasures are likely to be the
standard classics of the Union. People sniff
scornfully at the Brassey efforts to improve them,
‘ Shall you join the Govt. House reading circle?’
one of the Toorak women was asked. ‘ Well ’
(dubiously) ‘do they have refreshments after?’’
Refreshments are regarded as doubtful, and
society doesn’t bite at the off chance.
“ After the frolicsome and terpsichorean Hope-
toun, the ponderous Brassey is a fearful dead-
weight on all festive occasions. The women en-
deavor, to the utmost of their power, to persuade
him to give his dignity a holiday, but, so far, their
efforts have been a failure, and his early departure
is fervently prayed for. There is but one way
open to the clumsy millionaire to make himself
popular, and that is to respond to the request—
‘ Will Lord Brassey please oblige us with a skirt-
dance ? ’
The N.S.W. duty on cement comes off on
30th June, and the local cement-industry has been
killed. Goodlet and Smith’s cement-works were
closed last week and 100 men discharged.
Reid and Tozer, while each professing to
give the fullest credence to the other fellow’s yarn
about the Broken Hill trip, are both careful to
point out that the home-manufactured narrative is
much the more probable. In a contest of evidence,
however, Premier Reid, who admits that he is
occasionally a bit of a liar himself, has no chance
with Col.-Sec. Tozer, whose veracity is a house-
hold word in Kanakaland—so much so that he is
affectionately called “Truthful Tozer” by the
press, while in the backblocks the expression,
“You’re a Tozer ! ” is familiarly applied only to
local G. Washingtons. Besides, Tozer had a
letter to prove his story. Reid rather thinks the
proof wasn’t in the letter —which, of course, Tozer,
a most careless chap with written documents, des-
troyed right away. On the whole, the matter re-
mains in suspense, and the problems whether
Reid really did ask Tozer to come to Broken Hill;
why the N.S.W. Labor-members played two-
up with Tozer on the train, and merely funked
him before their constituents; and why they don’t
all wear tan boots like Griffiths, and live up to
them, must be handed down for posterity to
puzzle over.
N.S. Wales has bled scandalously over the
Dean Commission. F. W. Cumow (son of editor
Curnow, of S.M. Herald) was paid =£4l4 Is. for
acting as secretary and taking notes at the 39
sittings of the Dean Commission, or .£lO 12s. 4d.
per sitting (averaging, say, three hours in dura-
tion). Apparently he did not even transcribe his
notes for this, for transcription and typewriting
are charged extra ! T. D. Elwell, another ex-
Hekald reporter, who died in the course of the
sittings, received .£3lO for notes and transcription;
G. Elwell, who looks like a little brother Bill, drew
£22 for typewriting; C. F. Jones, another short-
hand writer, was paid =£244 for notes and tran-
scription (he seemingly did deceased Elwell’s work
just as well at half the screw); E. S. Garran (re-
lated to the ex-editor of S.M. Herald ?) got £l7 for
typewriting; and another £3O was divided among
miscellaneous typewriters. In addition, Mr. Cur-
now was paid £315 for making a precis of evidence,
so that his total cheque was £729 for work apparently
done between May 13, when the Commission first sat,
and June 28, when its report was presented— six
weeks work ! The total paid for clerical labor
was £1358 ; the lawyers and commissioners drew
£l5BO ; miscellaneous expenses, including printing,
were £491; and the abortive trial of Crick,
Meagher and the others for conspiracy cost £1123
—barrister Heydon drawing £715. The total cost
of Commission and conspiracy trial was £4553.
John Davies, the once-celebrated Sydney
political underground-engineer, died last week,
and thus follows his old master, Parkes, into
Shadowland at a respectful distance. Mr. Davies
began life as a blacksmith, dabbled in ward politics,
and developed into an all-powerful Parliamentary
organiser. He was smooth and sleek and oily and
black-coated, and travelled on the teetotal-and-
Orange racket. After having for years steadily
increased in power as Parkes’s Toby— or was
Parkes his Toby ?—he fell into ill odor over
some two or three little aldermanic and otnea-
matters and had to take refuge in the legis-
lative Council, where, though he was at nrs
almost boycotted, he ultimately became rattier
popular ; for he never forgot a favor or a face or a
promise, nor did he ever pass an old friend, now
ever shabby, in the street. John Davies was a
calculating man of strong common-sense, im l*?F'
turbable temper, and sphinx-like visage. In P u ° u
he spoke more to the point than lluan y bet
educated politicians, and he ever carefully dresse
his part ” according to nonconformist notions
Respectability—wherein lay no small portion o
his success. He was but 57 when he died—tr
the effects of an unaccountable complication
diseases.

Every man nowadays insists on getting a
full sovereign’s worth for each sovereign, and the
true business man’s policy is to meet every man
on that basis. No man in Australia acts more
stedfastly on this principle than does Mr. R. 0.
Hagon, the energetic merchant tailor of 129 King-
street, Sydney, and his establishment is, there-
fore, deservedly to the front in public esteem. The
new winter goods make a very fine display, and
while town patrons are invited to call and choose
for themselves, country ones are requested to drop
a line at once for patterns and self measurement
forms. The specially shrunken tweeds are a
feature which appeals to every man who thinks,
and all old cutomers can recommend prospective
new ones to invest their money in one of R. 0.
Hagon s suits. The establishment now employs
150 hands, and the chief cutter is the highest
salaried cutter in Sydney.*
The Rabbit Pest and How to Grapple with
It.— ln dealing with this question the ingenuity
of thousands of our colonists has been exerted to
find some means of stamping out the rabbit
plague. Fencing, trapping, poisoning, all have
been employed to arrest the increase of this pest,
but unfortunately the plague still makes progress,
and is surely extending through Queensland in
spite of all efforts to arrest its advance. The
people realise the enormous annual outlay of land-
owners in checking the increase of rabbits. The
sum spent every year exceeds £900,000. New
Zealand is the only colony, so far, that has
thoroughly reduced the plague ; the new poison,
“ Toxa, is the agent through which bunny is
destroyed by the million, and the New Zealander,
through it, has practically made rabbit-destruction
a paying business. The action of the poison is
instantaneous ; once a rabbit touches it he rarely
runs twenty yards, and all the skins are secured,
which more than defray the labor and cost of the
poison. It has been estimated that eight full-
grown rabbits will consume as much grass as a
full-grown shSep. The National Wool Company
introduced the poison to Australia, and where
tried it has given excellent results.*

In Australian cities and towns
no name calls up more agreeable
reminiscences than that of the Rev.
Chas. Clark, the King of Lecturers.
Clark was the pioneer of Smythe’s
Australian lecturing legion ; and he is
the best as well as the first. He is
now making his fourth Australian
tour; no other “celebrity” has ven-
tured on a second ; so Clark proves
again the theory of the survival of the
fittest. Hard work agrees with him.
Twenty-five years have passed since
his first appearance on the Melbourne
platform, where he lectured on be-
half of his church ; but age has
no more withered him than it
withers good wine. Clark was
trained as a Baptist minister, and
he is a Baptist minister still. Proba-
bly he thinks one denomination as
good as another ; for he preaches
from many_ pulpits, and has been
called the Universal Chaplain. Opi-
nions vary as to whether Clark ap-
pears at his best as a preacher or as a
lecturer. Some people prefer him in
the pulpit; for years after he arrived
in Melbourne you could not get into
his church, yet only last week it was
impossible to get into Melb. Town
Hall to hear him lecture. He must
have made a good deal of money ; but
he is ever ready to raise his voice in
the cause of charity. None who
gained admission will forget the sight
when, in Aug., 1890, he preached in
the Sydney Town Hall on behalf of
the city hospitals ; and there was a
similar scene in the Melb. Town Hall
last Sunday, when he preached in aid
of another charitable fund.
Hori Grey has been forbidden by his doctor
to pay his contemplated visit to Maoriland.
Parkes left his letters and papers in the
hands of B. R. Wise for possible publication.
Bevill is the N.S.W. grog-sellers’ secretary,
and Clark is the Great Panjandrum of the Cold
Tea crowd. A whisky-and-soda is now locally
known as “ a Bevill and Clark.”
Dr. Cullen, rival candidate with Speaker
Abbott for the vacant seat in the Senate of Sydney
University, was drafter of the Federation Bill
once fathered by Barton and Parkes.
Mr. Hugh Watt, lately accused by Premier
Kingston of having tried to bribe him in connec-
tion with a Northern Territory land-concession,
has been divorced at the instance of his wife.
Charles Wilson, brother to Salmon, died
at Cheltenham at Easter, aged 79 years. His
daughter was married, some two years ago, to Sir
George Baden Powell, who has since been living
in clover.
An aged Indian officer, with a brother in
the Scots Greys, is pushing a wheelbarrow round
Australia. So far, he has done 4000 miles, travel-
ling Q., N.S.W. and Vic., his only companion a
sheep-dog.
Many Sydney people will be glad to learn
that an erstwhile prominent bank-manager, whose
Australian career ended in disaster, has, in another
part of the world, by lucky speculations, made an
immense fortune.

Mr. Justice Simpson’s scheme of etiquette
covers matters far beyond the legal bar. He once
to’d a (now derelict) attorney that he should not
drink in public-houses : “Always wait till you get
to your club,” said his Honor.

that N.S.W. possessed 80 inhabitants, each with
an income of £lO,OOO and upwards a year. It is
said that the current income-tax returns reveal less
than 30 of these happy people.

Ho v these busted-bank directors love one another!

Another old Sydney identity, Mr. P. F.
Morgan, has died in London, aged 76 years. Mr.
Morgan was formerly in business as a chemist in
Sydney, and realised a handsome fortune. His
end was much hastened by worry arising out of
bank-reconstructions.
Dan Sapoo, a Cingalee, paralysed Justice
Cooper, the other day up North, by responding to
the question “ Are you a Christian ? ” “ No, I’m
a Wesleyan.” Cooper didn’t even smile, but cer-
tain of the saintly Rutledge’s enemies assert that
he went out and wept bitterly.
Superintendent Read is regarded by many
as the “natural” successor to N.S.W. Police
Inspector-General Fosbery, when that officer
chooses to retire. In this connection it is gravely
urged that the superintendent is a devoutly re-
ligious man who, in addition to fixedly maintain-
ing statute law, never misses an opportunity to
impress on policeman and prisoner alike that there
is a moral law higher than all. This characteristic
alone should win the billet from a “ God-fearing
Government.”
The death of Patrick Buckley has evoked
the statement that County Cork (Ireland) has
conferred perhaps more prominent men on Aus-
tralasia than has any other country or similar
division of territory in the United Kingdom. The
statement may be true. Besides Buckley, the
names of Martin, Madden and Stawell at once
rise to mind.

Poet J. Brunton Stephens has a sonnet
in the Queenslander which is a virtual reproach
against the Bananalanders for their apathy in
the matter of the Brunner catastrophe - fund.
Maoriland subscribed for the Q. flood-relief fund
,£13,000, and the donation was acknowledged in
some fine lines by the poet. He now asks shall
Maoriland “ mourn in vain ” ? Bananalanders
seem to be just going to let her mourn.
Among other letters received by Louis
Becke asking for information about the South Sea
Islands, was one from Maoriland asking him if a
well-educated girl could make a living by teaching
traders’ children. Becke is in a quandary how to
explain that traders’ children don’t all live under
the parental roof, and that there is no Brands
Act in Polynesia.
Mr. “ Banjo ” Paterson has at length been
persuaded to cease steeplechase riding, in which
congenial pastime he has experienced many narrow
escapes and some severe injuries. Of his last
eight cross-country races he won four, though on
no occasion did he ride the favorite.
Old R. H. Budd, famous in the annals of
Victorian education, kept an old-time school in
Victoria Parade, Melb. One of his pupils, says
Budd, was a reserved, shy little boy, meek and
docile. This particular brand of boy-angel de-
veloped later into the gentle Purves, Q.C.
Young “Charley” Martin, a son of the
late Chief-J ustice, has passed the preliminary ex-
amination for the English bar, and is about to
commence “eating his dinners.” If the young
man is anything like his brilliant father, eating
dinners should be an easy task.

to take the unusual step of having a “ Trial at

before a full bench as well as a jury. The Vic-
torian contracting case of Cornish v. Bruce, years
ago, was the most notable civil case of this nature
in Australia.

leviathan contractor, almost the Brassey of Aus-
tralia. He brought into the boom a mass of
capital wrung out of railway contracts, and yet
did not escape a painful overhauling, which
virtually slew him—for he had not the corky light-
ness of the farthing in the <£ man.
