innardness ”of things. The Northern public is
interestedly watching the Fowlers.
Henry Parkes, Burdett Smith, John
Davies, J. F. Burns, and George Withers were all
interested as part-proprietors, or agents, in the
Holt-Sutherland improvements, which were made
the subject of a Royal Commission a few years
ago, and now the three first-named are dead and
the remaining two are at legal war.
Agent-General Playford will return to S.A.
shortly owing to his wife’s ill-health. He means
to stand for the Leg. Council. It is just about
time Tom returned. Photos, have reached Adel-
aide of the ex-market-gardener rigged up like a
scarecrow in Court-dress, and with his large left
hand clutching a tin sword. Cheap cauliflower!
The “ severe abrasion ” of Gov. Brassey’s
nose through falling from his bike turned out to
be a very mild calamity. Brassey has the kind of
nose that would seem not much the worse for a
prize-fight. There’s nothing of the ancient Roman
about H.E.’s foremost feature, which harmonises
with his commonplace whiskers. Now, if Secre-
tary Wallington were to descend heavily on his
organ of smell !
Amongst the names on the Newcastle list
of volunteers for the proposed N.S.W. “Defence
Guard ” appears that of Thos. Maxwell Walsh—a
wiry-looking old fellow who gives his age as 74,
and his previous service as with the 70th Foot;
46th Foot; Military Train; Land Transport
Corps ; and Peshawur Light Horse. He holds
medals for the Crimean War, Indian Mutiny,
Sutlej Campaign, and Maori war.
Parkes once received a deputation of ladies
in N.S.W. Parliamentary Library. As they were
departing he took down a valuable book from the
shelves and handed it to the spokeswoman—a
well-known woman-suffragist—having first oblig-
ingly inscribed it—“ To Mrs. W , from Henry
Parkes.” Shortly afterwards, one of the members
requiring the book, its whereabouts was ascer-
tained and a messenger sent after it, who re-
claimed and brought it safely back.
Hon. W. Hart, the Tas. M.L.C. whose
name was allegedly forged upon a <£l2oo cheque
the other day, suffers from a presentiment that
he will die poor. He is one of the Tasmania
mine directors, a prominent Wesleyan, and oo-
trustee with manager Fitzgerald in the matter of
ijhe V.D.L. Bank lotteries. The Hon. Wm’s.
name doesn’t appear on the lottery ads. Fitz.
just signs “for self and co-trustee,” and the
M.L.C.’s conscience and lottery-hating church is
appeased.
The gloomy-looking J. P. Garvan, who,
when in N.S.W. Assembly, began most of his
speeches with, “ It is my sacred and solemn jooty,”
is to be major of the newly-formed N.S.W.
Irish Volunteer Brigade. So far, Mr. Garvan haa
been little, if at all, known in connection with
militarism, but, being a tiger to work, he may be
relied on to soon master his drill. A characteristic
story of him is that once being asked in his ex-
treme youth whether he would stay at home and
do sums or go to the circus he elected to do sums.
Which recalls the story of little Marcus Clarke
who, when asked by his white-haired maiden aunt
whether he would sing a hymn or have an orange,
replied “ Sing a hymn ” —and thereupon got two
oranges.
Begelhole the Great, erstwhile manager of
Bay ley’s Reward, turned up at North Fitzroy
(Melb.) police-station at 2 o’clock the other morn-
mg, telling a wondrous story about how he had
been chloroformed and robbed of <£2oo in cash and
jewellery by three men who had come from Melb.
with him per tram and alighted at the same time
to follow him to a dark spot. This was at 11,
explained the gallant captain, and he had not re-
covered consciousness for a couple of hours.
Begelhole can consider himself lucky. In some
places he would, while unconscious, have been
floated into a company of 200,000 shares, and
would have found on awaking that he had parted
with his immortal soul in exchange for 25,000 con-
tributing scrip.
Justice Simpson told barrister Armstrong
last week in court that it was not a barrister’s
duty to submit evidence simply because he was
instructed to do so ; on the contrary, that it was
a barrister’s duty to assist in the administration of
justice. The implication here is the same old one,
which the Dean-Meagher case should have deter-
mined as a rule of lawyer-life, but which the
courts have left to individual opinion as before.
Napoleon 1., when setting up his Commandments,
wrote on one table “ Advocates in law-courts shall
never speak but for the truth. ” But long ere he
started for Moscow he broke that table in two.
It means,” he said after, “ the death of the pro-
fession ; I might as well demand that soldiers
shall never fight except when on the side of
Right.”
The truth about little Dr. Maloney,
M.L.A., who has got himself in bad odour in
Melbourne by going on tour in India with
Richard, alias Geismardo, the “electric healer,”
shows up rather more to his credit than has been
represented. The facts related by those who know
the circumstances are that Dr. Maloney landed
himself in debt, through no fault of his own, but
through the treachery of a “ friend.” His medical
practice was large, but he never refused to attend
a poor patient for nothing, and in other cases those
who said they could not afford to pay were not
pressed. Richard offered him the chance of earn-
ing a big sum by doing genuine medical and
surgical work in company with his (Richard’s)
electric racket, and Maloney, seeing here a chance
of paying off his debts, accepted the offer. It is
so rare to find a Melbourne man making any sacri-
fice to pay his debts that the instance is note-
worthy. Maloney is coming back soon. He will
not resign his seat, but will resume polities and his
largely-charitable medical practice.

Osborne and Jerdan have taken over the
electrical, optical, and photographic busines*
formerly run by Frost and Shipham, and are carry-
ing it on at 438 George-st., Sydney. Nothing in
the way of an order comes amiss to the firm, from
a pair of specs, to a' mammoth tri-unial, or a
mechanical toy to a 50 - horse - power camera,
guaranteed O.K. and O.P. ; and anybody and
everybody is advised to write for the firm’s in-
teresting illustrated catalogue.*
INTERESTING TO LADY CYCLISTS
HICKS, ATKINSON & SONS,
Collins-st. , Melbourne,
Are now prepared to make, under Mr. B. King’s
special supervision, Tailor-made Cycling Jacket
and Patent Skirt of first-class durable tailoring
tweeds of the newest patterns, scientifically
cut stylishly made, from 4 guineas.
The Skirt is a special design by Mr. B. King,
and it is protected by Royal Letters Patent in
Victoria and N.S. Wales.
It is claimed for this Patent for small Cycling
Skirt (the first patent of the kind ever taken
out in the world) that it is now recognised and
thoroughly approved by the best and most
fashionable Lady’s Tailoring Firms in London
as giving, above all others, the greatest possible
satisfaction.
The main points of this Patent are :
1. Simplicity, modesty, and neatness.
2. Perfect safety from entanglement in the
machine.
3. Being small and scientifically cut, the
Skirt always retains its proper position ;
does not catch the wind so much as other
skirts, and yet gives ample room'and
perfect freedom for the limbs.
4. It is so constructed that it can be trans-
formed into an invisible divided skirt if
required.
Numerous testimonials expressing the highest
approval of B. King’s Patent Skirt have been
received.
HICKS, ATKINSON, & SONS.*
POLITICAL
POINTS
Two prominent Tories elected to S.A.
Assembly are likely to be unseated, owing to their
bolding b ; g Govt, contracts. One of them is said
to have transferred his contracts to his clerk.
Extract from the speech at the opening of
IS.S.W. Assembly : “ The strife in Parliament on
fiscal policy having terminated.” Sanguine Reid !
Phe “strife on fiscal policy” is not yet fairly
begun.
Affleck (N.S.W.) has a Bill in hand seeking
power to garnishee civil-servants. Three years
ago Crick, M.P., introduced a Bill which sought
to abolish garnishees of all sorts. Better Crick’s
Bill than Affleck’s.
N.S.W. Works Minister Young will have
a tough time at next general election, and so will
the “God-fearing” Education Minister Garrard,
whose troubles will give him a memorable chance
of testing the efficacy of prayer.
To save Manchester from the bankruptcy
threatened by the serpent bites of its own beloved
“ free competition,” Lowles, M.P., backed by the
Argus and the Imperial Federation League of
Victoria, is proposing to shut up the Manchester
school.
A return giving the names of all who carry
the gold free-pass for life on N. S. W. railways
would, it is said, be most interesting. Ministers
of four years’ standing are entitled to it, officials
say, but the question is—Are these the only
wearers ?
Premier Reid says that the rumor that
one of his Ministers is about to leave the Govern-
ment is “ wild and foundationless.” It would, in-
deed, be a wild thing for anyone of the calibre of
Cook, Garrard, or Young to leave 1500 lovely
sovereigns a year.
Is Mr. Simpson, who asks for statutory
power to build a bridge over Sydney Harbour, the
gentleman who got a Parliamentary concession
some years ago to build a certain railway in the
Parramatta district ? And if he is, did he or any-
one else ever build that railway ?
Melbourne Age item: “Michael Munza,
George Schroeder, Leo de Hardt, Heinrich Helm-
quist, William Soderquist, and Andrew Johnston,
all seamen on board the British ship Westward
Ho, were brought before the City Court yesterday
charged with disobeying orders,” &c. Five mis-
cellaneous Scandinavians and a Scotchman ! The
British seaman !
Expected that “Turncoat Bill” Copley
will lead the Ass party in S.A. Assembly. This
is the man who introduced the Free Education
and Payment of Members Bills into S.A. Council,
and is now elected as a pronounced opponent of
the same measures ! The Ass party in the Assem-
bly, by the way, numbers about 16, and there
are at least six candidates for the leadership.
Some time ago a gang of Kanakas on the
Herbert were unloading manure from Torrens
Creek meat-works. The overseer found them lazy
and proceeded to stir them up. Hereupon the
islanders “ struck.” “ Too much stinkum,” they
complained; and when the manager arrived the
dusky spokesman pleaded, “ Guv’ment no likem
this sort work longa Kanak ; he white man work.’’
“ I am in favor of one vote for manhood
and one for thrift,” said candidate Tom Bent at
S. Melb. Gall —astounding gall! Bent, the
arch-boomster, part-author of the financial reign
of terror and of the preceding inflation that
flourished on the savings of the thrifty, posing as
an apostle of thrift, an encourager of thrift, an
advocate of frugality and money-hoarding on the
part of the workers !
The special meeting of the Trades Hall
body whose duty it was to convrm or reject the
S. Melb. Labor League’s nomination for that
much-wooed electorate, was called by an obscure
advt. in one paper on the morning of the meeting.
Many members did not know of it till next day.
As the meeting was fought with closed doors, the
whole thing has as unpleasant an odor about it
as anything else in this grossly-mismanaged elec-
tion.
Tactics at a certain Queensland polling-
place—or perhaps several of them. Two com-
partments for voters; one is provided with
blotting-paper, the other isn’t. The Democratic
voter, whenever recognised, is sent into the com-
partment where there is no blotting-paper. Con-
sequently he, in most cases, folds up his paper
with the ink still wet, and the ink spreads over the
paper and makes the vote illegible. Rejected as
informal.
William Gaunson, David’s little brother
Bill, stood for Melb. City Council the other day,
and was licked by a seven-to-one majority. Little
Bill Gaunson nowadays stands for pretty nearly
every Parliamentary or City Council or other
vacancy, and the voters throw him out with pain-
ful regularity, and he comes back again next time
to be thrown out some more. Every dull thud
that is heard anyw r here in Victoria now is assumed
off-hand to be the bump of W. Gaunson’s head as
he is thrown out. After his latest defeat W.G.
announced that “he felt it his duty to contest
every vacancy,” and he was going to do it regard-
less of consequences. The statement was received
with scorn by an audience which refused to regard
Gaunson as a real individual. It is an awful thing
to have no individuality of your own, and to be
simply somebody’s brother Bill.
Bent’s appearance as a candidate created
profound disgust in South Melbourne, where the
electors roll up manfully to the meetings of the
Brighton Boomer, and raise Sheol about his large,
purple ears. South Melbourne feels hurt, and if
Thomas doesn’t lose his £5O
it will be an astonishing and
disgraceful thing.
Nowadays a Q. deputa-
tion asks the visiting Minis-
ter two things : Will he pro-
vide a clock for the local
Post-office tower ? and will
he build a lazaret for the
local lepers ?
Frank Madden, M.L.A.,
that specially Heaven-born
member of a loquacious
Smellbourne family, has
found a Cause for the “ de-
generacy or complete lack of
Australian national charac-
ter.” Socialism is the cause
—vile, disgraceful Socialism,
which prompts any half-
dozen of boys who club to-
gether, whether for football
or cricket purposes, to pester
distinguished public persons
for subscriptions.” Madden,
who evidently speaks as a
d.p.p., is confusing Socialism
with Tory crawlsomeness. A
true Socialist is a good
Democrat, and a good Demo-
crat doesn’t seek unwilling
contributions to any sort of
club. The boys who pester
Madden are mostly the de-
generate offspring of the
snobs who send him to Par-
liament.

back of the Truck Bill, now ushered into notice for
the severalth time, should, while at it, make the
measure cover in unequivocal words the deductions
made by hosts of employers on the score of sick-
pay t.e., pay for prospective medical attendance,

truck Act,
worded so as to defeat ingenious devices, and it is
not regarded as perfect even now.

At the recent election the Ass crowd worked the
Act for all it was worth, the same elector in many

Democracy and orthodox religion don’t
love one another any too well—especially in the
North. The “ godless ” Towers returns two
Labor men ; “godly ” Townsville puts in two typi-
cal Fat Men. In the West and North the sound
of the church bell doesn’t peal out, in some dis-
tricts, once in six months —and the men are the
most democratic in Australia. Said a Labor
M.L.A. some time ago : “ There’s neither church
nor chapel, priest nor parson in my electorate—
and I hope there never will be.”
ABOVE SUSPICION
BOSS COOKIE : “ Look here, Gecko, that dog of yours has been
worrying my sheep again. "
GECKO (very “civilised”): “No, no — mine dog longa tima bin one
vegetarian.”
Premier Reid’s offer of moral support to
Great Britain during the Transvaal trouble calls
up a curious inconsistency in the advocates of Im-
perialism. The stock argument for the “eonnec-
tion” and attachment to the throne, &c., is the abso-
lute necessity of Australia being defended by Bri-
tain. But on the first flutter of diplomatic trouble,
Britain is rushed with offers of assistance from “the
colonies,” and wild speeches of promise from Pre-
miers and other loyal persons. If Britain is strong
enough to protect us, is it not babyish to offer her
aid • If she is not, and wants our assistance,
what chance have we ? The question is-—ls
Britain our big b. other Bill ? Or are we Britain’s
big brother Bill ? Or are both parties equally Bill?
It is not only by the free-carriage of
newspapers themselves that N.S. Wales loses
a big revenue. Journals often cover letters,
and such light articles as samples of dress-
material, laces, ribbons, etc. Women, the
authorities find, are the greatest offenders;
they will take great risks to save post-
age. A while back the Sydney P.O. officials found
a long letter hidden in the folds of a paper, plain-
tively asking addressee, “ Why don’t you answer
my letters ? ” and containing the confession, “this
is the third letter this week that I have sent you
in the S.M. Herald.” " The authorities were able
to trace the writer of that letter because she—it
was a lady, as usual—gave name and address.
But the revenue-defrauders rarely give themselves
away thus. All the authorities can do when they
discover surreptitiously-inserted matter is to keep
back the papers and confiscate the enclosures.
But as the papers, etc., are usually carried to their
destination before being searched—if they are
searched at all—the country has to bear the cost
of carriage just the same. N.S.W. and Tasmania
are the only countries in the world that quietly
submit to the free-postage steal.
Why the Boer loves the Briton. In 1815
there was a Boer rising in the Eastern province
as a protest against the British annexation’,
which was suppressed with great severity. Five
of the leaders were condemned to be hanged, and
the sentence was carried out before a large’ con-
course of people. Unfortunately, the gallows
broke down under the weight of the five, and the
hapless men, reviving, crawled to the feet of the
commanding-officer and prayed for mercy. .
At sunset, they obtained all there was to spare'
and were formally hanged again. . . . Sounds
like a chapter out of Botany Bay.
Some Queanbeyan (N.S.W.)electioneering
tactics. At last election, friends of Palmer, the
Freetrade candidate, tired of raising a futile
sectarian cry against E. W. O’Sullivan, resorted
to the dodge of writing to the old Irish farmers
stating that their man was approved of by Card.
Moran, and that the local priest had got instruc-
tions to secure his return. Did O’Sullivan resent
that dodge ? Not much. He got hold of one of
the letters and had it printed and posted to all the
ultra-Protestants of the district. Result—the
Protestants, believing that Palmer might really
be the Cardinal’s nominee, polled for the Holy
Roman, who actually beat Palmer in his native
district by a large majority.
Tom Bent didn’t know he was politically de-
funct till last week. The ex-Speaker reckoned that
the moral and physical support of wrestler Victor
would duly impress the muscular Christians of
Sowth Melbourne, and get a hearing for him, but
Tom’s calculations were ridiculed by the result.
The meeting howled unanimously at Tom, and
heaped scorn on Mons. V. when that faded athlete
attempted to rally round the orator. The discom-
fiture of both bull-necked gentlemen was complete.
Bent gave the cue for Victor to say something,
and the baldheaded Frenchman fired off a protest.
Everything depended on the reception given to
Victor, who was promptly routed by the voice of
one crying from the body of the hall—“ Get your
’air cut. ”
The modest John Tighe Ryan in Gun-
dag a i Times on the N.S.W. Governor’s speech
to Parliament :
From a literary point of view, this State clocuiir nt is
perhaps the worst ever printed, and it is not too much to
say that it is a disgrace to the province. Even the refer-
ence to the death of Sir Henry Parkes is not made in
correct English, and the veteran statesman is robbed of
the titles conferred upon him by the Queen. In an
ordinary communication or newspaper article this would,
of course, be overlooked, but it is a breach of courtesy
and an ignorance of that gracious ceremony which should
characterise State documents, in a speech by the Gover-
nor at the opening of Parliament, of which he was for
many years the guiding light and the principal adorn-
ment. We are given to understand, however, in slipshod
language that the children of the statesman have a claim
upon the national gratitude.
This is the first time The Bulletin has learned
that any Governor was ever a “ guiding light and
principal adornment ” of Parliament. It is to be
hoped that Premier Reid (who, by the way, did
not continue to Mr. J. Tighe Ryan the free-pass
oyer all the N.S.W. railways granted by Dibbs) will
digest the grammatical lesson from so eminent an
authority.
The French Minister of Agriculture reports
that 23,000 horses, mules, and donkeys were
slaughtered for human consumption in Paris last
year. One-third of the horse-meat was sold at
butchers’ stalls, and the remainder cropped up in
“ bags of mystery.”
The name of “ E. Dyson ” at the foot of a
yarn or a bit of verse is familiar to all readers of
The Bulletin, and is generally accepted as a
guarantee that something well worth study lies
above it. Mr. Dyson’s book of verse, entitled
“ Rhymes from the Mines and Other Lines,” is to
be published in style by Angus and Robertson
next month, and will be supplied direct from this
office.

John Hunter, boot-manufacturer and im-
porter, has 70 establishments altogether in N.S.-W.
and Queensland, with large factories at Sydney
and Brisbane. He employs over 1 00 hands, and,
besides his country brandies, he has salaried
travellers and commission -men who regularly do
the country. He went lately to London, where he
was called “The Australian Boot King,” and he
established a house there to deal direct with the
manufacturers of London, Paris, and Vienna, so
as to put goods on the Australian market at lowest
possible prices. And from London he brought the
latest bootmaking machinery, so as to manufacture
here on the most economical lines. He is now also
developing his mercery business, the stock of
which, in the wholesale department alone, repre-
sents £30,000 —very considerably less, however,
than the boot stock. The Strand Arcade Mercery
Bazaar is his; the well and favorably-known
mercery business of J. C. Eley, in Hunter-street,
has lately become his ; he has also annexed the 50
years’ established business of Smith and Mannel,
shirtmakers, mercers, &c. These the enterprising
John Hunter will shortly open in his own name,
with a big discount-sale to lend eclat to the occa-
sion. He has more mercery-shops at Broken Hill,
Parramatta, Newcastle, &c. ; he has 12 grindery-
shops in Sydney and Brisbane; he has lately
opened a new and handsome bootshop in King-
street, Sydney ; and he is going to increase and
improve his City Boot Palace. Further, he will
shortly move from his present warehouse into one
of the largest warehouses in Sydney.*
The helpless way in which the average
man or woman potters along with obsolete means
and methods is one of the saddest features of Aus-
tralian semi-civilisation. There is no experiment,
no initiative, no seeking for better things among
our people. The importance of diet to brain,
bone, and muscle is regarded by dull and stupid
intellects as a fad ; they do not get within miles of
the idea that the stomach is the last organ to trifle
with ; they never seem to realise that just as poison
taken into the stomach kills, so wholesome food
produces health and strength. The winter is now
on us, and porridge is the order for breakfast.
Those people who want to know what makes the
healthiest porridge should remember the name
Gillespie’s Flaked Grainea. *
Computed that in London there are at
least 50,000 families so poor as to have but one
room per family.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
The early-betting publio got a crumpler through
hot quarries goinsr out of S.T.C. Royal Stakes and Cups.
Forrester scratched the favorite, Milkmaid, for the shorter
race, standing by Lord Rosebery, and some unfeeling
brutes weren’t sorry when he ran last. Akarini’s win repre-
sented something good to her heavy-plunging stable.
The filly’s alliterative combination, Mentor-Mitrailleuse,
makes her one of the best-bred in the land. Her dam
was one of Maoriland’s fastest flyers. Two-year-old Huret
was conspicuous near home, and ran third, the missing-
link being Patrona. Any of half a dozen would have won,
so close up were they, but for the winner.
As chasers, the 10 entrants for S. T. Club’s
steeple weren’t very safe conveyances. Seven either fell
or refused. Othello, the M.L. horse, who has been
dodging round for something easy, smothered Grandwing
in the final sprint. He was unfancied. Thor’s hurdles
victory was a greater surprise. Beaten off at Rosehill
just previously, he made snails of his field on this occa-
sion.
When a stable gets a lightly-weighted old ’un in
a race, it doesn’t have to finesse much for a market, if he
belongs to Buckley’s division. Cooee, with 6.8, w’on
S. T, Club Handicap from start to finish, landing a cosy
pile for his party. Punters never even thought of such an
unpretentious crock.
Gov. Buxton looked like a fish out of water on
the last day of Adelaide Cup meeting. The V.R. party
was huddled up in the grand-stand all the afternoon,
except when H.E. inspected the totalisator, and the sad-
ness of the occasion was only relieved by the offer of a
cheeky “ tipster” to put His Ex. on to a “dead snip” for
the Steeple.
The biggest divs. at Adelaide R.C. were in two
minor selling-races—£22 9s. and £4B 6s.
Mostyn and Destiny landed a handsome double
for R. Barr Smith in City Handicap and Cup, paying £9
3s. and £l9 11s. respectively. Mostyn is a great weight-
packer over a short course. Destiny, who downed a swell
field, has always had “ expectations,” but Hova’s last
wasn’t the champion’s true form.
Trentham made no impression in Adelaide Birth-
day Cup. He appears to want a spell. St. Albans’ could
only land a small stake at the meet with the fine-looking
Koran.
• * * *
,£3OO to £lOO against Newhaven for V.R.C.
Derby was booked to a leading Adelaide sport last week.
Big risk for the money, and he may get the price three
months hence.
• * * *
Noted that big clubs generally caution pro-
minent jocks., who have been cautioned before, instead of
passing them out. Saddle cracks seem to think they can
do as they like because they ride for big owners. Their
calculations don’t go very wide of the mark. Influence
means immunity.
* * * *
Adelaide is a sure refuge for all the bookies who
have had to take hurried flight from Sydney and Mel-
bourne. Disqualification of a “ book ”in Melb." applies to
Sydney, and vice-versa, but Adelaide does not reciprocate,
and prominent S.A. sports are urging a move in that
direction. At present Adelaide shelters more shady pen-
cillers than any other Australian city, despite the totali-
sator. Destitute of cash for straight-out wagering, most
of these fiends are double-eventers. The first event for
backers is to strike the double, and the second to collect
the money.
* * * *
Arrived at last. Dunedin (M.L.) club now
brackets an owner’s string and pays out if one scores.
The public should relish the innovation, which goes far to
abolish hanky-panky. How would Ikey Mo like to
pay out two prads for the one investment ?
* * * ♦
Phil. Glenister says Humphrey Oxenham is the
biggest bettor Australia ever had, Joe Thompson included.
* * * *
“Thediv. paid on Nomadic (in the S.A.J.C.
Totalisator Handicap), £39 55., was one of the ‘ healthy
sort,’ and will provide The Bulletin with another argu-
ment in favor of the tote against the bookmaker, albeit
the latter will ever remain a necessary to the turf,” says
Adelaide Standard. Nonsense. Just as the starting-
machine is superseding the jockey-fining man with the
flag, so the legalised betting-machine must in the end
down and abolish the short-odds-laying Ikey Mos.
* * * *
A Melb. sporting pen critic wails about the
tote’s grasping 10 per cent, deduction policy. But he
omits to mention that the percentage is devoted to in-
creasing stake-values. Ikey Mo increases nothing, so far
as is visible, except his stomach and his bank-balance.
* * * *
M.L. people are already getting a foretaste of
what will happen should Premier Seddon kick down the
tote next session. At all tote-prohibited country meet-
ings spielers and mushroom bookies welsh and balance on
every hand. Punters must bet, and, if there’s no machine,
so much the worse for the bettors. Backing losers causes
much vexation of spirit, but backing winners and getting
no div. is an unutterable thing.
* * * *
Another gamble-inducing firm has sent out its
probable-Cup-nomination list, and odds against each. One
mentioned beauty was the other day tried among gallo-
ways. Another now trots his owner to church.
* * * *
At a Sydney club last week a prominent sport
solemnly averred that next Melb. Cup would be won by a
hurdler.
* * • *
Towers (Q.) pressmen have an eye to the main
chance. Reporters told local club-members at a general
meeting that one race entrant had been baptised with beer
“ for advertising purposes,” and that if people wanted to
advertise their beer they could do so at scale-rates. It
was suggested that the prad be re-christened Gingerbeer.
* * * »
Not improbable that measures will soon be taken
to make all persons dealt with by clubs for cronk prac-
tices amenable to criminal law also. The great cronk
crowd could then pace round the gaol-yard in broken
time, and sleep on the premises.
• * • •
In addition to ordinary consultations, a Johan-
nesburg promoter has a ten-thousand-pounder on Epsom
Derby ; divided amongst first three only.
* * * *
Sydney “ silver ” betting biz is a worse nuisance
than it was when “ shops ” were unrestricted. Then
people walked in, made their bets, and departed in peace.
Now,' one cannot go window-gazing in certain streets
without being asked by “gents of the class” if he fane’es
anything. The exemptions are females, parsons, and
peelers, a doubtful betting trio—in public, at all events.
• * * *
The latest Australian definition of a rowing
“amateur” excludes bookmakers. Fancy Ikey Me pull-
ing himself blind in an eight-thwart racing shell!
* * * *
Champion has as heading for his racing-col r n
“ The Quick and the Dead.” Good.
* * * »
The Australians won their Crystal Palace m:.t :h
against an “Eleven of England” on their merits un-
doubtedly, but the crushing completeness of the victory
really seems to need explanation. The London press Lad
written disparagingly of our men, and the “gates ” were
likely to suffer in consequence. The Englishmen, mostly
professionals with no incentive to distinguish themselves
in a scratch-match of this sort, must surely have played a
very loose game. On paper it was about the most re-
markable feat ever achieved by an Australian Eleven
abroad. Fine weather, wicket “lively” all the time.
Englishmen (winning the toss), 114 and 39; Australians,
374 —an innings and 221 runs to spare. Previous to which
they had done only fairly well against Essex, a second-
rate county.
Cyclist O’Hehir, the Ballarat top-notch road
rider, is a bench-hand at a sawmill. Years ago air the
fingers of his left hand were cut off, and a few months
back he unfortunately lost three from his right fist. Yet
he is riding again with his old-time pluok and vigor.
A cheerless day, lumpy water, crossing steamers,
and other assorted oddments of bad luck turned the inter-
provincial rowing spins into a mermaids’ picnic. Vic. won
the fours by XJ lengths from M.L., after fouling the latter,
a set of young giants, whose boat was lowest in the water,
and could have been sent along faster if its crew feathered
higher. The islanders were handicap}, ed by bad steering,
too, and bow nearly “crabbed ” once through viewing the
dismal scenery. N.S.W. men were the worst watermen.
Referee Simpson unwisely gave his decision on M.L.’s pro-
test before hearing the evidence, admitting afterwards
that he hadn’t clearly seen all that transpired. Champion
Slack (Q.) had an uneasy, but decisive, win in the
sculls from Bannister (N.S.W.) M‘Cormack (M.L.), third,
amply demonstrated near the finish that he was a fast
calm-water puller.
» * * *
Wanted a new cricket rule for cases like this :
Katanning and Broomihill C.C.’s were playing at Katan-
ning, W.A. ; batsman struck ball hard and full to leg, it
struck the back of the umpire, rebounded towards striker’s
wicket, and was caught at short-leg. The assaulted um-
pire, on appeal, ruled “ caught out.”
* * * *
A disgusted Englishman wouldn’t stop to see
the Grecian Olympian games. “ The king and suite, and
3000 people, daily turned out to watch the men training,
and cheered lustily when one cove jumped only sft. 2in.”
• * * *
Melb. Herald : —“ Christy marked and trilbied
Melbourne’s fourth goal.” Poor Trilby !
« * * *
Up till now not one fair-sized crowd has col-
lected this season at a Melb. football-match.
* * * *
Felix, Jun.: “The Australian physique is evinced
by the fact that, so far as I know, none of our cricketers
sent to England are dead, unless Midwinter be included.”
* * * *
Sporting papers constantly refer to Chandler’s
famous jump of 39ft. over water at Warwick. The gentle-
man, Col. Harrison Broadley, who rode him on that occa-
sion, has just died. He was a grand old fellow, and a
great friend of the late Tom Crommelin, one of the finest
sportsmen of the century, who died in Sydney. Mr.
Crommelin saw the horse make the celebrated leap, and
he essayed to equal it in cold blood the next day, and
very nearly succeeded, his old horse Waterloo (or Welling-
ton) covering 36ft. over a brook.
* * * *
A Melb. correspondent :—“ Re your remark
apropos of Interprovincial Eight-Oar Race, ‘great is
George Upward ’; it is but fair to state that Irving was
the only man who received any coaching from him.
Five of the others got all their tuition from Sid. Edwards,
of boat-building fame. 'As for the ‘ hall-mark of Geo.
Upward,’ which appeared in the Age a week ago, that is
utter nonsense.” —Fair Play.
• * * •
Phil. Glenister, the veteran turf-commissioner,
lately grew reminiscent in Adelaide over Melb. Cup in ’BB.
Mentor, he says, won £20,000 for the owner and 1:30,000
for Glenister and his friends. When Chester won the Cup
in ’79, Phil, backed Savanaka, the favorite, for £43,000
without leaving the room, in one night. First Water,
second in ’B3, was backed for £60,000 in Sydney alone.
Carbine won £40,000 altogether, one wager laid to Wallace
being £21,800 to £lO2O. Even Nada was backed for
£33,000. “ Somehow or other,” says Phil., “Wilson, of
St. Albans, seems to have no luck in Melb. Cup. Carnage,
Strathmore, and Nada all ran into places, and the latter
would have won had he not been interfered with.”
* * * *
W. T. Jones swears by Anwin, who won Melb.
Cup for him on Bravo, beating Carbine, and has engaged
him to ride Newhaven in liis future races. Also, he may
go ’Ome when the colt goes.
* *' * *
No wonder silver double-books pay ! In a (say)
£lO to Is. chance, the same odds rule all round, the
rankest weeds and the choicest plums being booked at the
same price. There are always backers of even the worst,
too, especially when it’s thought the best aren’t taking
any.
* * * *
An old-time M.L. sport-contributor, possessing
excellent horsey judgment but wretched grammatical
qualifications, arranged with the turf-ignorant “ sub.” to
elaborate his notes. The partnership flourished until
one day the “elaborator” went into ecstacies over the
performances of a well-bred gelding, whose probable great
success at the stud he lefigthily dilated upon. The much-
lauglied-at and greatly-shamed contributor entered the
office groggy, threw down his cigar, and expressed his
feelings in fistic fashion. During the fight the cigar set
fire to the littered room, and the brigade had to be rung
out. The man of tips resigned right away, and the next
day the paper came out with a fiery article, headed “ The
Curse of Horse-racing.”
* * * *
The high-jumping Mazeppa was once a cab-
horse ; dragged a wood-and-water cart, and a vegetable
van ; was originally a whim-turner, and is now, perhaps,
the best high-jumper in Australia.
* * * *
Ika, a minor Steeple-winner in M.L., is out of
a draught mare. His sire got Kingfish, whose 1.40 for
Hawkesbury (N.S.W.) Guineas has never been equalled.
* ♦ » »
A. H. Holder, jumping badly, recently cut
down the M.L. amateur quarter-mile hurdle record, held
by “Wairarapa” Roberts (Auckland), to 60sec., after
winning a lOOyds.-race from scratch in 10 l-ssec. Holder,
a one-time pro., stood down for two years, and was offi-
cially “ whitewashed.”
♦ * * *
Marmion, the ex-steeplechaser, is now running
in a Sydney boot-manufacturer’s cart.
* * * *
A N.S.W. up-country football crowd objected
to play its opponents when the time came because a
colored man had a place in the team. Unique in latter
day Australian athleticism. A heavy, stoushing, ebony-
visaged black certainly is objectionable—if he’s not on
your side. A great many think football a niggers’
game, anyway.
* * * *
A diminutive pony-riding Sydney nonentity
joined the Salvation Army the other day, on the off
chance of getting something to eat occasionally.
* * * *
If pony-clubs offered substantial monetary prizes
to the jocks, who rode most winners at their meets within
a given time, the sudden resuscitation of dead-’uns occa-
sionally would surprise owners as much as the public.
* * * *
Redleap won for the Messrs. Miller all the
money expended on the handsome stables that they have
named after him. The stables are, in fact, a monument
to the memory of the great ’chaser, who, in the M.’s
opinion, might have run away with a Melb. Cup if they
could have brought him fit to the post. Redleap, they
say, was never really well but once, when he won the
l’lemington G.N. Hurdle Race with a trifle over 9st. on
his back and any amount of weight in hand. He went
amiss before the Steeplechase of a week later, which fell
to Eaglet, their “ second string.” Two years later the big
un was sufficiently fit to win the double event, Hurdle
and Steeple, followed shortly by the four miles’ Steeple at
Caulfield. After which it was impossible to train him.

“ Slippery Jack,” the N.S.W. bush-thief
who has been caught at last, after three years’ de-
fiance of the police, is a Spaniard, and a New
Caledonian escapee.


The Public Service Board’s recent investi-
gation of N.S. W. Education Department resulted,
for one thing, in the Board away armed with
a list containing 40 names of officials found guilty
of being over the regulation age of 60. It is dis-
cretionary with the Board to either retrench or
retain all these officials. But when they reach 65
the Act leaves no choice but retrenchment.

“Pet” Names in Court.
Melbourne Insolvency Court is a sordid place where
simple expressions of conjugal love are held ridiculous,
ine other day, when a turf was under
examination, counsel started to read one of insolvent’s
letters to his wife, beginning, “ My darling Snoozer,” and
the Court broke out into loud laughter. Yet there was a
world of poetic suggestion in that pet name. The Bulle-
tin finds it positively inspiring. Music fills the office air,
and a gentle flapping of invisible plumes seem to fan our
heated fancies :
Guard her, angels, while she sleeps,
Nigh her pillow hover ;
Dry the tear she, dreaming, weeps
For her husband-lover.
Honey from her lips I’d take,
Like a thirsty boozer,
Were it not unkind to wake
My own darling Snoozer.
Fair the form that lies at rest,
Fair each facial feature ;
Nature grants no whiter breast
To a human creature.
Were all women mine to woo,
Could I be a chooser
Of a sweeter spouse than you ?
No, my darling Snoozer.
Guard her, angels, night and day,
Peace and comfort give her
Till her spirit wings away
To the shining river.
When she draws her final breath
Say not I must lose her,
Let me share the sleep of death
With my darling Snoozer.
Rabbit-lies from the Never-Never. Up-
river man to Down-river man : “ Our rabbits—
why, our rabbits leap fences like cats.” Down-
river man : “ Ours roost on ’em like fowls.” Up-
river man : “ if ours ain’t started to gnaw
through the rabbit-netting to get in to water.”
Down-river man : “ Oh, we poison the wires, and
darned if they haven’t started to root out the posts
now.”
An S.A. farmer, noticing that white ants
don’t attack posts set up in salt swamps, has
solved the ant-problem by putting into each post-
hole, when filled within 6in. of the surface, a
shovelful of salt, and completing with soil as
before. The salt impregnates the subsoil water
round the posts, which rises and so salts the posts
effectually. In saltlake country the posts can
simply be soaked in the lakes for a few months.
Extract from the land-tax schedule of a
farmer located about sixty miles from Sydney :
I have 45 acres of land. The unimproved value is nil,
as the cost of the improvements I have made is three
times as much as the present selling-value of the land and
improvements combined, and the 5 per cent, interest paid
to the Government on the balance owing is more than the
rental value of the whole property. There are thousands
of acres of Government land adjoining me, east and
south, that is unimproved except with my dividing-fence.
This land has been open for sale for the last 30 years, at
the usual £1 per acre, without getting a single bid. The
above presents a picture of farm life after 30 j-ears of toil,
from 12 to 16 hours per day, contending with floods,
droughts, fires, and low markets. The Government has
taken its lb. of flesh out of it, and the mortgage (or death-
grip) will have to be handed down to posterity. The only
thing left me is the bone, and I am seriously thinking of
letting the Government and the mortgagee' fight for the
bone, as I am simply working for nix to grease these two
old fat sows. There is no unearned increment on the
land, and never has been. Perhaps Premier Reid will find
us billets in the Civil service.

At Young and Jackson’s (Melb.) last week.
Very fat-cheeked man (respectably dressed) after
swigging off his whisky, is reminded that it is not
paid for. Searches pockets and declares he has
changed his coat and has no money, but will go
back to office and pay directly. “ Look here, sir,”
says the barman, “ don’t go back. Just sell that
cheek of yours as pig at fourpence a pound, and
I’ll let you have three bottles of fizz for the pro-
ceeds. ” Fact.
A GRAND LITERARY INSTITUTION.
Cole’s Book Arcade now runs from the centre
of the block in Bourke-street to the centre of the
block in Collins-street, Melb., a distance of G6oft.,
and contains the following 17 departments, mostly
literary and artistic : 1, New Books ; 2, Second-
hand Books ; 3, Stationery ; 4, Music ; 5, Fancy
Goods ; 6, Toys ; 7, Perfumery; 8, Pictures ; 9,
Artists’ Materials ; 10, an Ornamental Exhibition ;
11, a Japanese Exhibition ; 12, a Wonderland ;
13, a Smiling Gallery; 14, a Child’s Picture
Gallery ; 15, a Bargain Room ; 16, a Tea Room ;
17, a splendidly arranged new Wholesale Book
Department, containing half-a-million books. The
main walks of the establishment are half-a-mile
long. *

A new poem has lately been discovered
among some Hindu MSS. unearthed at Benares
(India). The author, Ali Muhibb Khan, was the
Laureate of the day, and his masterpiece, brought
to light after a few centuries of seclusion, suggests
a new poetical theme. It is entitled “ Khatmal
Baisi, or Twenty-two Stanzas in praise of the
Bug ” ! The preface perorates to this effect: —•
“ Tigers fear him—snakes and elephants, the
mightiest kings on earth, heaven and hell fly
before him—nay Death himself when he descends
to carry away a fleeting soul refuses to approach
the sick man’s bed if it be infested by the mighty
birnex !” A hint to Alfred Austin.
Henry Lawson’s Poems.—Post-free from
Bulletin office, ss. sd. in stamps or postal notes
of any Australasian province.
Tasmania badly wants police centralisation.
Territorial police are more or less under State
control, while the municipal Robert generally
manages to control the municipality. The police-
superintendent of a rural municipality has all the
powers of a Crown prosecutor, with necessarily
few qualifications for the position ; the municipal
warden has all the powers of a R. M. with still
fewer qualifications for the billet than the Superin-
tendent has for his. One depends largely on the
other for continuance in office, and the combina-
tion constitutes a double-barrelled monarchy of
mutual interests which exercises almost despotic
power in the administration of justice, and plays
high jinks with the morals of the community.
The other day a whole family were arraigned on a
charge of arson, their furniture, and even their
clothing, was forcibly taken possession of by the
police Superintendent, and every possible legal
indignity was heaped upon the suspects prior to
their committal for trial. The Crown Law
authorities, powerless to interfere with the local
magnates earlier, characterised the prosecution as
a persecution, and the Attorney-General refused
to file a bill. Local Government as applied to the
police force finds its reductio ad absardum, in
Tasmania, where a man on the rampage can evade
arrest by simply crossing a two-chain road into
another district when the locally-appointed bobby
heaves in sight. The policeman dare not cross the
boundary to effect an arrest, and the evil-doer
may, with impunity, hurl blue-metal at the
majesty of the law across the street, and pass rude
remarks concerning the size of the force’s feet.

The Land Board (subject, of course, to
appeal) has brought down the rent of Netley
station, on the Darling above Wentworth, N.S.W.
from 'Bld. to '3Bd. On this holding it is estimated
that the rabbits have reduced the carrying capacity
by more than GO per cent., notwithstanding that
the average cost of rabbit-destruction has been
.£670 per annum for 10 years. In the last five
years the average losses of sheep have been 15,000
a year. Of 21,000 lambs in 1895, only 3000 were
shorn, and this year 18,000 sheep have been lost
since the stock-returns went in. Add to this that
there is poor prospect of any lambing this season,
that the price of wool and stock has fallen, say'
33 per cent, net in 10 years, and you begin to get
an idea of what squatting at its worst is. Or,
rather, perhaps, since the owners can still more
than keep their heads above water, you get an idea
of what squatting was at its best.
The men of Malayta show a fine capacity
for civilisation. On ‘the labor-schooner Sybil,
when about to leave Maryborough, were found
some 20 rifles and revolvers,' about 1000 cartridges,
and six or seven pounds of dynamite.
THE AUSTRALIAN’GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Ehzabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
quyer of all other Australian gems.*
A junior Sydney barrister was moving the
other day for a rule nisi for a new trial in an action
for damages caused to plaintiff’s property by the
spilling of nightsoil thereon, through the negli-
gence of defendant’s servants. The Court
pointed out that the error committed at the first
trial would easily be rectified at the second, and
that applicant’s client must eventually be unsuc-
cessful ; he was, therefore, asked whether it was
worth while proceeding. “ But surely,” came the
answer, “ they cannot recover if I prove it was an
act of God.” Spilling nightsoil an act of God!

A Micronesian Bade Debt.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One day a Sydney vessel, loaded with
cocoa-nut oil and sandal-wood and bound for
China, appeared off the little island of Pingelap, in
the Caroline Group. At the time referred to the
sandalwood trade was carried on by ships whose
crews were assemblages of the most utter ruffians in
the Pacific Ocean, and this brigantine was no excep-
tion. There may have been grades of villainy in
the crew; perhaps if any one of them was more
blood-stained and criminal than the others it was
her captain.
There being no anchorage at Pingelap, the cap-
tain sailed in as close as he dared, and then hove-
to, waiting for the natives to come aboard with
some turtle. Presently a canoe shoved off, filled
with natives. As she pulled up alongside, the
captain glanced at the white man who was steer-
ing and his face paled. He turned quickly away
and went below.
* * * *
The mate of the sandal-wooder shook
hands with the white man and looked curiously at
him. Only by his speech could he be recog-
nised as an Englishman. His hair —long, rough and
black—fell on his shoulders like that of a native, A
white duck jumper was his only article of Euro-
pean clothing ; round his loins was a native girdle
of beaten cocoa-nut leaves. And his skin was as
dark as that of his savage native crew- -lie looked
and was a true Micronesian beachcomber.
“ You’re under mighty short canvas, my friend,”
said the mate of the vessel by way of pleasantry.
The man with the brown skin turned on him
savagely.
“ What the h —— is that to you ? I don’t dress
to please a pack of —— convicts and cut-throats.
Do you want to buy any turtle—that’s the ques-
tion. And where’s the captain ?■”
“Captain Watson has gone below sick, sir,”
said the steward, coming up and speaking to the
mate. “ He says not to wait for the turtle but to
fill away again. ”
“Can’t,” said the mate, sharply. “Tell him
there isn’t enough wind. What’s the matter with
him ? ”
“ Don’t know, sir. Only said lie was took bad
sudden.”
With an oath expressive of disgust the mate
turned to the beachcomber. “ You’ve had your
trouble for nothing, you see. The old man won’t
wait—why, what the h is wrong with you ? ”
* * * *
The bearded, savage-looking being was
leaning against a backstay, his hands tightly
clenched and his eyes fixed in a wild, insane stare.
He straightened himself up and spoke with an
effort.
“Nothing. I’m all right now. ’Tis a fearful
hot day, and the sun has giddied me a bit. But
gettin’ the turtle won’t delay you. I want tobacco
badly. You can have as many turtle as you want
for a couple of pounds o’ tobacco. ”
“Right,” said the mate, “that’s dirt-cheap.
Get ’em aboard as quick as you can. Let’s have
twenty,”
The beachcomber laughed. “You don’t know
much about Pingelap turtle if you think a canoe
would hold one. We’ve got ’em here five hundred-
lead)’ Y 0U ’ U haVe to send a boat. 111 So on
as horn! °k ° Ver the side into the canoe and paddled
The mate went below to tell the skipper.

meat badly. .Shall I lower the boat ? ”

comes back.”
* * * *
1 lie second mate and his crew followed the
whll e U A aU f nd , a crowd of natives to the pond
where the turtles were kept. It was merely a
huge pool m the reef, with a rough wall of coral
slabs built round to prevent the turtle escaping.
Add good idea—— ” commenced the second
mate, when there was a lightning rush of the
Drown-skmned men upon him and his crew. At
mocking a man down and tying him up securely,
your Caroline Islander is unmatched.
• * * * *
lt "P“ 8 rather sudden, isn’t it, Barton ? ”
I ne beachcomber was speaking to him, looking
into His eyes as he lay upon the ground. “You
don t remember my face, do you ? Perhaps my
back would improve your memory.”
a A cr ? s 8 the sullen features of the seaman there
flashed a quick light—the gleam of a memory.
But ms time was brief. The beachcomber whis-
pered to a native. A heavy stone was lashed to
the mate s chest. Then they dropped him over
the wall into the pond. The native sailors they
left where they lay.
And now ensued a hurried, whispered colloquy.
I he story of that day’s work is not yet forgotten
among the old hands of Poriape and Yap. .Suffice
it to say that by a cunningly-contrived device the
captain was led to believe that the second mate
and his crew had deserted, and sent the chief mate
and six native sailors to aid the natives in recap-
turing them. The presence of numbers of women
and children walking unconcernedly about the
beach made him assured that no treachery vVas in-
tended. The mate and his men were captured in
one of the houses, where they had been taken by
the beachcomber for a drink. They were seized
from behind and at once bound, but without any
unnecessarily rough usage.
“ What’s all this for?” said the mate uncon-
cernedly to the white man, He was an old hand
and thought it meant a heavy ransom—or death.
The beach-comber was standing outside in the
blazing sun, looking at tile ship. There were a
number of natives on board selling fish and young
cocoanuts. The women and children still saun-
tered to and fro on the beach. He entered the
house and answered the query.
“It means this—no harm to you and these six
men here if you lie quiet and wait till I send for
you to come aboard again. The other four men
are alive but tied up.”
“Ah,” said the mate, “ I see, you mean to cut
off the ship.”
“ No, I don’t. But I have an old debt to settle
with the skipper. Keep quiet or you’ll follow
Mister Barton. He’s dead. ”
Then the beachcomber, with some twenty natives,
went to where the first four men were lying and
carried them down into the mate’s boat.
» » * »
“Here’s the second mate’s chaps, sir,”
said the carpenter, “ the natives has ’em tied hand
and foot like pigs. But I don’t see Barton among
’em.”
“No,” said the captain, “ they wouldn’t tie up
a white man. He’ll come off with Willis and the
turtle. I never thought Barton would bolt.”
The ruse succeeded admirably. The boat-load
of natives had hardly been ten seconds on deck ere
the brigantine was captured. Watson, lashed in a
sitting position to the quarter railing, saw the last
man of the cutting-out party step on board, and a
deadly fear seized him. For that last man was
the beachcomber.
He walked aft and stood over him. “ Come on
board, Captain Thomas Watson,” he said, mock-
ingly saluting him. Then he stepped back and
surveyed his prisoner.
“ You look well, Watson. You know me now,
don’t you ? ”
The red, bloated face of the ski pper patched and
mottled and his breath came in quick, short gasps
of rage and terror.
“ Ah, of course you do ! Three years ago since
that Sunday at Vate when you had me triced up
and Barton peeled the hide off me in strips. You
said I’d never forget it and I’ve come to tell you
that you were right. I haven’t.
He stopped. His white teeth glistened beneath
the black-bearded lips in a low laugh, a laugh that
chilled the soul of his listener.
A light air rippled the water and tilled the sails,
and the brigantine moved. The man went to the
wheel and gave it a turn to port.
* * ♦ »
“Yes,” he resumed, casting his eye aloft,
“ I’m delighted to have a talk with you, Watson.
You will see that your crew are working the ship
for me. You don't mind, do you, eh ? And we
can talk a bit, can’t we, Watson ? ”
No answer came.
“None of the old hands left, I see, Watson—
except Barton. Do you know where he is now ?
No ? He’s dead. I hadn’t any particular grudge
against him. He was only your flogger. But I
killed him, and I’m going to kill you.” He
crossed his bare, sinewy arms on the wheel, and
smiled again at the bound and terrified wretch,
“You’ve had new bulwarks and spars since, I
see. Making money fast now, I suppose. I hope
your mate is a good navigator, Watson. He’s
going to take this ship to China.”
Then the fear-stricken man found his tongue,
and a wild, gasping appeal for mercy broke from
him.
“Don’t murder me, Hollis. I’ve been a bad
man all my life. For God’s sake, let me off ! I
Was a brute to you. I’ve got a wife and children.
For Christ’s sake *! ”
The man sprang from the wheel and kicked him
savagely in the mouth with his bare foot.
“Ha ! you done it now. ‘For Christ’s sake—
for Christ’s sake ! ’ Don’t you remember when I
used those words : ‘ For Christ’s sake, sir, hear
me ! I did not run away. I got lost coming from
the place where we were cutting the wood.’” A
flicker of foam fell on his tawny hand. “You
dog, you bloody-minded fiend ! For three years I
have waited .... and I have you now.”
A choking groan of agony came from Watson.
“Hollis! Spare me .... my children!”
The man had gone back to the wheel, calm
again. His seaman’s eye ran aloft, and the wheel
spun round. “Ready, about!” he called. The
brigantine went about and stood in again—to meet
the mate’s boat.
* * * *
“Come this way, Mr. Willis. Captain
Watson and I have been having a chat about old
times. You don’t know me, do you? Captain
Watson is a little upset just now, so I’ll tell you
who I am. My name is Hollis. I was one of the
hands of this ship. I am owner now. Funny,
isn’t it ? Now, now ; don’t get excited, Mr. Willis,
and look about you in that way. There isn’t a
ghost of a chance; I can tell you that. If you
made one step towards me, you and every man
Jack would have his throat cut. And as soon as
I have finished my business with our friend here
you’ll be captain—and owner, too, if you like.
By the bye, what’s the cargo worth ? ”
The mate told him.
* ♦ * *
“ Ah, quite a nice little sum—two thousand
pounds. Now, Mr. Willis, that will be practically
yours. With only one other white man on board,
you can take the vessel to Honolulu and sell both
her and the cargo, and no questions asked. Hard
on our friend here, though ; isn’t it ? ”
“ Good God, man, what are you going to do to
the captain—murder him ? ”
“ For God’s sake, Willis, help me ! ” The mute
agony in the skipper’s face, more than the spoken
words, moved even the rough and brutal nature of
the mate, and he opened his lips to speak.
“No ! ” hissed the man at the wheel; “ not to
save my soul from hell. Look at this ! ”
He tore off his canvas jumper, and presented
his scarred and hideous back to the mate.
“ Now, listen to me, Mr. Willis. Go below and
pass up as much tobacco and trade as will fill the
small boat. I don’t want plunder ; but these
natives of mine do.”
In a few minutes the goods were hoisted up and
lowered into the boat. The two six-pounders on
the main-deck were run overboard, and all the
small arms taken from the cabin by the natives.
“ Call your men aft,” the white man said to
Willis. They came along the deck and stood
behind him.
“ Carry that man on to the main hatch.”
Two of the strongest of the native sailors picked
up the burly figure of the captain and laid him on
the spot the beachcomber indicated, and cut his
bonds.
* * * *
A dead silence. The tall, sun-baked figure
of the sinewy beachcomber, naked save for his grass
girdle, seemed as he stood at the wheel, the only
animate thing on board. He raised his finger and
beckoned to a sailor to come and steer. Then
with quick strides he reached the hatch and stood
in front of his prey.
“ Captain Tom Watson. Look at me well ;
and see what you have made me. Your time
. . . and mine ... at last. ”
He extended his hand. A native placed in it
the hilt of a knife, short, broad-bladed, heavy, and
keen-edged.
“Hal Can’t you speak? Can’t you say ‘for
Christ’s sake ’ ? Don’t the words stick in your
throat ? ”
The sinewy left hand darted out and seized the
fated man by the hair, and then with a savage
backward jerk bent back his head and drew taut
the skin of the coarse, thick throat. There was a
flash of steel ....
* * * ♦
He wiped the knife on his girdle, and

looked in silence at the bubbling arterial stream
that poured down over the hatch-coamings.
“ You won’t forget my name, will you?” he said
to the mate. “Hollis; Hollis, of Sydney; they
know me there ; the man that was flogged at Yate
and left ashore to die at Santo.” _
He glanced down at the limp, huddled-up
mass at his feet, got into the boat, and with his
naked associates paddled ashore.
The breeze had freshened up, and as the brigan-
tine slowly sailed past the orowded native huts a
hundred yards distant, the mate saw him standing
by his thatched house watching.
A native girl came up to him with a wooden
water-bowl, and stood waiting. With his eyes
still fixed on the ship he thrust in his reddened
hands, moved them to and fro, then drawing them
out, raised them shoulder-high, dashed them down
again and went inside.
LOUIS BECKE.
‘ 1 If you have a pound you cannot be the
friend of the man who has only a penny.”
“ Princess ” Beatrice is to be elevated to
the peerage as Duchess of Kent. It may not be
generally known that it is “sheer vulgarity ” to
call the younger children of a British sovereign
“princes” and “princesses” until they are pre-
sumably raised to the peerage. They are “ com-
moners,” and, probably, in theory, could be pro-
ceeded against for using titles without proper
warrant,
‘ ‘ Fortunately, the ambitious and grasping
aims of millionaires do not rise above the disten-
sion of their money-bags. Napoleons, Washing-
tons, and Garibaldis, men who sWay the destinies
of mankind, are not found in the ranks of million-
aires,” moralises Melb, Age over the coffin of
Baron Hirsch ! The temperament of a Washing-
ton or a Garibaldi wouldn’t have allowed him to
preserve a lot of money, oven though gold had
dropped into his coffers from the clouds. A ttian
of noble aspirations can’t do the things necessary
to the amassing of an enormous fortune, or die
very rich if he inherits one—not unless the legacy
comes to him in his last moments. The suggestion
that a millionaire might become a, higher order of
being if he made an effort is irrational. How
high could his spirit soar under the weight of a
ton of bullion?
At Fremantle (W.A.) shipping affairs have
long been in a hopeless muddle. Steamers unload
at a jetty where they can find room— i.e. , they lie
at the jetty and wait for railway trucks. After a
time two or three trucks come along and, having
been loaded up, run their cargoes off the jetty and
dump them on a spot called “ the farm,” where
they lie in the sun and rain until the railway
department can carry them to their destination—
generally some weeks, or it may be months, later.
Recently timber ships have been making rafts of
their cargoes and landing them, on the beach.
There was much excitement recently when a
steamer arrived at the port with a 15-ton boiler for
a crushing works on board. It could not be
lightered, and all the govt, cranes at the jetty con-
fessed themselves unequal to lifting it. Finally
all the holes were plugged up and the boiler was
thrown overboard and anchored in the roads,
where it stayed for some days looking like an iron
whale. Occasionally a scare would be raised by
the report that it was sinking. But this did not
happen, the boiler being ultimately towed ashore
safely. Fremantle is a very good place for skip-
pers to stay away from.

The two richest men in Berlin pay income
tax on .£150,000 and <£103,000 per annum respec-
tively—quite small potatoes compared with the
millionaires of London and New York. Never
theless, the Arbeiter Zeitung complains that
while these persons are so uncommonly well-to-do
“ three-fourths of the Prussian nation live in a
miserable condition, many thousands are hungry
and hundreds die annually of starvation.” Some
newspapers are always grumbling,
A correspondent
Dear Bulletin,— Please note that Sydney Telegraph
which championed (1) the tramway grabsters and (2) the
North Shore-bridge grabsters, now champions (3) the
Wyalong grabsters. Whenever you see a slashing article
in the Telegraph wanting to sell the public some noble
reform cheap, ask the Telegraph “ Whv ? ” The Wyalorw
idea is obviously syndicate-snavelling and London flotation
on the basis of last year’s London returns. If the Wva
long mine-owners want that, let them have it. But do
they want it, or is the scheme only one in which two or
three owners and two or three broking-sharks are con
cerned? The system on which Wvalong has been
run has worked well, but it has always been
foreseen that the claims would have to be amalgamated as
shafts got deeper. Now that the Mines Dept, has decided
to permit amalgamation, there is nothing to prevent
claim-holders from joining their forces at once, employ-
ing the same amount of labor, and dividing the profits
among themselves as hitherto. But the D.T. clamors for
“ more area’’—why ? Because the London speculator is
used to tiie 25-acre or bigger Westralian leases, and it
isn’t much use trying him with 2 acres or 3 acres of Wva-
long ground, however rich, for the £lOO,OOO co which
the sharks are after. But 2 acres or 3 acres will pay the
present holders to work themselves, and keep the profits
on the field and in the country. The Mines Dept, deserve*
credit, not blame, for trying to shield the local working
miner from the big foreign syndicate.— Bed-Rock.
An Old-Time Horse-Soldiering
Case.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Brogg was making a forced journey from
Jindabo to Camptown—some 250 miles —in “ the
old days.” He relied on the “ horse-tailers -
whom everybody up to snuff patronised in those
times, or got their horses “ bushed ” if they didn’t
—“to provide him with a horse (“square” or
“cross”) at each stage en route, and an un-
doubtedly “square” horse for the final fly into
Camptown.
Camptown then consisted of one private build-
ing with pretentions to size—partly pub., partly
store, partly butcher’s shop, and partly black-
smith’s forge; a few humpies; also, a police-station
and court-house in one, and a police-paddock. A
Chinaman ran a market-garden some three miles
away. Camptown boasted a police magistrate,
who was several other official things as well, and
who lived at the pub. Most of the other residents
ate at the pub., too, but lived in tents, or slept
pillowed on their saddles in the open, save where
bark shelters had been erected. The pub. veran-
dah on a wet night was crowded, heads and legs,
with sleepers. Barring blacks, there were not
half-a-dozen women at Camptown.
At the 12-mile waterhole, Brogg got his last
horse from the tailers —a fine-shouldered grey. He
looked at the brands and at the moke.
“Say, is this O.K. ? Never saw him before.
Whose is he ? ”
“Dunno,” replied the tailers, “But it’s all
square. He comes from the other end, and ain’t
known here at all. ”
“The other end” meant nearly 200 miles the
other side of Jindabo, so Brogg mounted the grey
with an easy conscience, and cantered gaily into
Camptown early in the afternoon. He hung the
grey to a ring in the post in front of the pub, and
sang out for Billy, the groom and taker-down
generally.
Entering the pub., he encountered the police-
magistrate and the sergeant of police, hailed them
as old mates, and ordered drinks.
Suddenly there was a hubbub outside. The trio
went to see. Billy had the Chinese cabbage-man
by the back of the neck, and was threatening
awful things in lurid language. The Chinky was
holding on to the grey horse’s bridle.
“ Sahjint! Thatee my ho’se. Thatee been stole
sixee munse. Who bling him ? ”
Says Brogg : “ That’s my horse, you jumped-up
Chow-liar. ”
“ Sahjint! Me gotee leceet. You lookee blands.
All li ! Me givee this man in cha’ge.”
“By G — ! Brogg,” says the police-magistrate,
in an aside ; “ this is serious. Where did you get
the moke ? ”
“ Erom the (blanky) tailers. They said it was
from the other end. What’s to be done ? ”
“Hum ! If the Chow swears an informa-
tion, I’ll have to take notice of it. You’ll be
arrested. . . . Sergeant! Is the receipt all
right ? ”
“Eaith, Oi’m afraid it is, and the Chow wants
a warrant.”
A warrant was issued, Brogg was formally
arrested, and allowed out (on his own bail) tiil
next morning.
Then he and the P.M. and the sergeant and
Bailey (also a Government official in a minor way
—did I say Brogg was a Govt, official? no? well,
he was !) started to make a night of it —liquor,
cards, and dice.

Brogg did so. Bandy raised his eyebrow for a

liquors under heaven mixed in a lemonade-tumbler
and livened up with a whisk; it contained also a
teaspoonful of tincture of cayenne, and was war-
ranted to make you comfortably drunk all day.

Tbe NEW “LIP-FLAN<SE” FRUE VANNER BELT.
“ Will OOtlast Two of tfec Ordiijary.”
Genuine “ Frue ” Belts (obtainable only from
the patentees or their authorised agents) have, owing
to the great care and attention paid to their manu-
facture, invariably given good satisfaction, but, after
being in use a long time, the old flange had a ten-
dency to become weak and lose its vertical position.
This prevented the machine carrying the proper depth
of load, and allowed the sand to slop over and get
into the bearings. The new “ Lip-Flange,” which
V '•
SECTIONAL VIEW OF FLANGE.
has just been patented throughout the colonies, does
away with this difficulty almost entirely. It is
made of a very superior and elastic rubber prepared
in a special manner, and its peculiar shape prevents
it cracking in going over the rollers, or losing its
vertical position. All “ Frue Vanners ” sold in the
future will be fitted with this new “Lip-Flange”
Belt without additional charge, and the belts them-
selves may be procured from the patentees at a very
slight advance on the old price.
